RATING: 8.5
A game of 5 rounds where you use dice as an action limiter, and attempt to score the most points in a variety of ways.  The theme of travelling Asia is present but definitely thin, there is significant variability in gameplay due to setup and asymmetric characters, and it has a definite "I want to play again" factor to it (likely due to the relative shortness and variability of gameplay).

The game is for 2-4 players (it scales very well), it lasts 5 rounds, and each round the players roll their personal 5 dice of their player colour.  These will largely dictate what actions they will select with their dice that particular round.  On a players turn, they do one and only one main action but they have the option of doing multiple bonus actions before and/or after their main action.  The actions are: 1) collect money (at best it's 5 gold), 2) go the market, where there are several goods that can be "purchased" (camels, pepper, silk, and gold, listed here in increasing value and difficulty to obtain), 3) earn the favour of the Khan, which is a simpler lower payout version of the market in essence, 4) obtain 1 to 2 new contracts (these are where you will get a lot of your points and are primarily why you need goods from the market), and 5) go travelling, which costs money and is related to the map which also often requires further tolls of camels or money (this is the other main way to score points).

Typically you will want higher dice as higher dice generally give better/stronger actions. There are some instances however, when lower dice are good; whenever a player chooses an action with their dice, other players may still choose this action but they will then have to pay in gold, therefore if you are taking an action late you will get it cheaper with low dice values, also some actions do not benefit from high dice values (earning 5 gold, often the favour of Khan, and sometimes travelling).  As well at the beginning when players roll their dice there is a small gold compensation for very low roll totals (this is very minor however).

The bonus actions are as follows: 1) re-roll a die (thus costs a camel per re-roll), 2) move a due value up or down by one (this costs 2 camels, and unfortunately you cannot wrap around), 3) earn 3 gold (this is unlimited, you do not have to pay for taking it late, but it does use up a precious action die), 4) obtain a bonus black die for the round (this costs 3 camels, and these are both powerful and limited, powerful as it's an extra die and it also allows a lot of placement freedom with the die as you cannot normally place more than one die of the same colour at the same location, but with black die you can).

The majority of points are obtained from: 1) completing contracts which are typically a mix of camels and marketplace goods and which pay out points but also potentially other goods, black die, more contracts, or free movement, and 2) movement on the map of Asia. This is an integral but difficult part of the game, as movement is very costly and the routes you want to take are often mutually exclusive to each other.  When you move into a city, you automatically get to place one of your trading posts in that city and cities come in 2 flavours small and big.  Small cities pay out an income at the start of every round, and big cities provide new die action spaces for those players who reached that city and many of these city actions provide scoring options (there are only a small number of city cards used each round from a large stack in the game box), as well as a small one-time bonus for the first player to reach that city.  You also want to travel as at the start of the game you will secretly select 2 travel cards which pay out end game points for reaching certain cities and for reaching a large number of different cities.  As well, if you can get down all of your trading posts it's worth a bonus 15 end game points and if you can reach the city of Beijing it's worth up to 10 endgame points (and it also allows you to convert left over resources into points which are otherwise worthless).

The most interesting aspect to the game is that each player will get 1 unique player character with a powerful ability and a lot of the fun and replay ability comes in trying out new characters each game. 

Own the [boardgame=186554]New Characters[/boardgame] mini-expansion.

RATING: 6.5
Some established mechanisms mixed and with some new twists could have made for a nice worker placement game.
But the characters seems unbalanced, und so the whole game. Just getting goods and fulfilling contracts is quite easy and mostly successful (especiall with mercator), and mostly the player who almost ignores the travelling (except a few easy moves), which is suppoed to be the core of the game, wins.
Among players who have played only few games, that is. The game may turn out to be well balanced among experienced players, but it is not among beginners in this game. Furthermore it leaves a bad feeling, that the easiest way to be succesful is imore or less ignoring the whole travelling part, and simply getting goods and selling them through contracts, so reducing the game to a very simple stupid game.

RATING: 8
7 plays / 3,4 players

LIKE: The individual player powers break the game and make it very interesting. Lots of choices and not many rounds to do it, keeps the game tense and interesting.

DISLIKE: Some player powers are boring. Analysis paralysis will stall the game because there are combos abound, especially late game. Lots of iconography, but after one play it gets easier.

OVERALL: This is the type of Euro I enjoy most. There are no unique mechanisms, it's all standard resource conversion, but the presentation and game breaking characters make the game exciting and tense. My only worry is that because there is little player interaction, not sure how much replayability is here. So far, it keeps getting played.

RATING: 6
The Voyages of Marco Polo will definitely appeal to everyone who like their nuts to be tough to crack. By this I mean that in the the game you experience an acute shortage of everything. You are always one resource, money or action short from executing all what you want. And this is not only at the end of the game, the pressure is constant. As a gaming experience this pressure translates to an unforgiving optimisation exercise of the use of resources and actions. You simply cannot afford to waste anything if you want to have that sensation of accomplishment. Should your steps stray from the optimal path you quickly find yourself in a deadlock from where there is no escape. 

When it comes to other aspects of the game, I find it quite interesting. Dice as workers to place is a nice mechanic and forces you to adapt to a degree since you cannot plan everything ahead. But the best thing in my opinion are the strong special powers each character and city grants. They really seem to make a difference. This creates myriad of valid strategies which, from what I've experienced and heard, are quite well balanced. This is truly a great achievement. So many ways to play, so many ways to win, you just have to find your own niche where there is less competition. However, the strong powers also add to the need for heavy optimisation. One cannot forgo their powers, since generalists who do a little bit of everything will not excel. To make the best use of your powers you have direct your scarce resources on a single goal, while pessimised moves gain you nothing, absolutely nothing.

Marco Polo has a steep learning curve and provides a subject to study for a long time. I like puzzles and I like tough games. But I reckon The Voyages of Marco Polo might be best played online, although not in a live match. This is simply because the ruthlessness of the game does not reward casual play. Personally, I do not enjoy sloppy plays, but at the same time, I do not like being the person who is APing in the table. Similarly, I detest sitting in a table where one or several players are  not even trying to win or freeze all the time (admittedly I am often guilty of paralysing myself, so it won't be me throwing the first rock and thus I am not complaining too much). 

A game that has a very similar feel and tickles the same cell in your brains is Terra Mystica, IMHO.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
+ The New Characters.

This is an amazing game, that play after play is becoming one of my favorites. First of all the production quality is top notch for a euro-game. The custom wooden components are really nice, the cardboard is thick and sturdy, the artwork is functional but also aesthetically pleasing. Overall the game has just an intriguing look when it's on the table. Going beyond the surface, the mechanisms in the Voyages of Marco Polo may not seem very innovative when taken individually, but the sum of its parts creates instead a very interesting and unique game-play. There's SO much to like in this "mix of mechanisms"!

I like that the board is divided in two sections, where your dice/workers follow different rules. I like the concept of unlocking new actions by traveling on the map. I like the fact that every time - given the enormous amount of cards in the box - those actions can create cool combos that you never saw before. I like this huge variety, that forces you every time to pay attention to those action cards, to the interactions between them and to their position on the board, before you build your strategy. Yet what I like the most about Marco Polo is its overpowered nature, which is also probably the most innovative element of the entire game. It's not just the characters, which are all very different and absolutely powerful, but a lot of the city actions are incredibly strong too!

For this very reason though it's not uncommon to find some mixed opinions about this game. Many people questioning its balance, whether is that particular character or the travel strategy or the contract strategy. Yet, while I can see how some people might get these impressions when one player starts making a ton of points out of some combo, I don't see this as a lack of balance myself instead. After all that's what the game is about for me: the exploitation of the strongest powers in that particular game. Depending on the actions that come out during the set-up and where they come out some strategy might be easier or stronger that the others, but then it's up to the player to find it before someone else at the table does. That's the beauty of Marco Polo!

Overall The Voyages of Marco Polo is an absolutely amazing game with beautiful components, that can offer a great deal of different challenges and re-playability... and I find myself loving it more and more as the time goes by. I highly recommend it to every euro-gamer out there!

RATING: 8.85
Believe the hype! This is a marvelous game with unending replayability. I have only two quibbles with it:
-while the rule-book is great, and the player aids are helpful, I do wish reminders had been added to the player aids about the "extra" costs for the various actions.
-not too surprisingly, VMP is very AP prone, which can lead to major problems. How?  A player can use up all of his dice before the others, and sits out the rest of his round. If he has one or more AP-prone players still playing that round, the player may have a long, tedious wait before there's anything to do again! You've been warned.

For a euro, Voyages of Marco Polo is very well-themed. I don't know what it is with Italian designers, but they seem to have a talent for wedding historical theme with mechanics.

RATING: 8
The last few years have been lean times for me and the eurogame, all the more so for me and Hans im Gluck.  The last game of theirs that has stayed in my collection is Egizia from 2009.  At first glance The Voyages of Marco Polo didn't look promising.  The board has a clutter which suggests that Rococo style of modern euro design where even the bells and whistles have bells and whistles and the points all have salads.  Happily, in this case looks are deceptive.

The goals in Marco Polo are quite straightforward; fulfil contracts and establish trading posts in places that secret objective cards require.  Marco Polo's interest lies in how you go about these things using the special abilities of a character and of cities where you have established trading houses, and in what you prioritise.  What drives the game is a system of dice placement that rewards being first to select each action.  The secret objectives, characters and city bonuses are all variable so this isn't a game that is solveable.  What's more, while there is no direct player interaction, the action selections of other players will certainly have you recalculating your priorities again and again. 

The gaudy box excepted, the artwork is quite good.  Production quality is decent, although the die-cutting could have been better and the self assembly box insert is pretty crap.  Judging by the size of the rules forum, we weren't the only ones left with questions.

RATING: 7.5
Άλλο ένα eurogame με ζάρια και γενικό theme εμπορίου. Οι παίκτες μαζεύουν υλικά (χρυσό, μετάξι, μπαχάρια και καμήλες) και ολοκληρώνουν συμβόλαια για να μαζέψουν πόντους. Παράλληλα υπάρχει και ένας χάρτης στο ταμπλό, πάνω στον οποίο οι παίκτες ταξιδεύουν και οι πόλεις που επισκέπτεσαι είτε σου δίνουν κάποιο μπόνους εισόδημα είτε κάποιο καινούργιο action space για να βάλεις τα ζάρια σου. Όλα αυτά τοποθετούνται στο χάρτη τυχαία στην αρχή του παιχνιδιού, δίνοντας του έτσι πόντους στο replayability του.

Τα ζάρια μπορείς να τα κάνεις reroll πληρώνοντας μια καμήλα ή να το κάνεις +/- 1 πληρώνοντας 2 καμήλες. Όμως όταν μπορείς να αγοράσεις νέο ζάρι με 3 καμήλες δεν καταλαβαίνω γιατί να ασχοληθεί κανείς με το απλό +1. Άλλωστε οι καμήλες είναι απαραίτητες και για τα συμβόλαια και για τα ταξίδια στο χάρτη.

Γενικά το παιχνίδι είναι σφιχτό, τα χρήματα δεν σου φτάνουν ποτέ καθώς τα ξοδεύεις συνέχεια είτε για να μετακινηθείς στο χάρτη, είτε για να πας σε κάποιο action space που είναι πιασμένο από άλλον. Ξέχασα να πω πως ναι μεν το παιχνίδι είναι worker placement με ζάρια αλλά αν πληρώσεις τότε μπορείς να μπεις και εσύ στο μέρος που θες.

Τελικά αυτό που κάνει το παιχνίδι να ξεχωρίζει είναι πως ο κάθε παίκτης παίρνει στην αρχή έναν χαρακτήρα με μια μοναδική ικανότητα. Όλες οι ικανότητες είναι σπασμένες. Τόσο σπασμένες που είναι σαν να παίζει ο καθένας άλλο παιχνίδι. Δεν έχω ιδέα αν οι χαρακτήρες είναι ισορροπημένοι μεταξύ τους. Ελπίζω να είναι.

Καλή ποιότητα παραγωγής αν και δεν είμαι φαν της ιδέας να χρησιμοποιούνται 2 μεγέθη meeples για τα resources. Πάντα μπερδεύομαι την ώρα που παίζουμε. modest Συνολικά πας πήρε λίγο λιγότερο από 2 ώρες για μια παρτίδα τεσσάρων παικτών.

Θέλω να το ξαναπαίξω για να δω και άλλους χαρακτήρες σε δράση

RATING: 6

As much as I liked the mechanisms, I'm in no hurry to play this again and wouldn't recommend it to anyone else.

RATING: 7
This is a nicely designed euro where you roll your five dice, then in player order, use them as workers on board spaces that provide you with resources, travel camels, or money, that can then be used to fulfill victory point/extra benefits contracts. or allow you to travel across a board to spaces which also earn you benefits, often immediately, and at the beginning of subsequent rounds.  

Once a worker space is taken, subsequent players can use that space, but the subsequent use costs extra money, which seems to be scarce in the game.   The worker placement aspect of the game worked fairly nicely in our four player game, but I tried very hard to always be first to place each round.  I did not like paying those extra costs!

The addition of seemingly game breaking asymmetrical powers for each player, drafted at the beginning of the game, definitely adds spice or interest to the game, predictably encouraging die hard euro gamers to test out each seemingly wild power over the course of several games.  

Thee powers were nice to play with, but in retrospect, they seemed a bit like adding catnip for euro gamers who are curiously and doggedly determined to prove that certain powers clearly break the game.  I do wonder:  could the rest of the game stand on its own, be of interest over repeated plays, without these asymmetrical powers?  Not sure.

Lastly, I won my first game by going very heavy on fulfilling contracts to the detriment of moving on the board.  I seemed to be the only player doing this.  Some in the comments here have suggested that this "fulfill contracts" approach is too powerful.  After only one play, all I can say is maybe.  Fulfilling contracts seemed to me to be the central purpose of the game (similar to MANY euros), so I am not quite certain as to why winning the game by fulfilling the purpose of the game is a problem.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Marco Polo is a game that could have been much better. Problem is that it isn't very balanced and it is also a little too random. Contracts strategy is way easier than travel and, regrettably, also more boring. The character powers could be great, but it's clear that some are much better than others and can really shift the balance in favour of some players. Some characters are totally dependent on map layout to be successful and most of the time the random map just does not get generated to make much sense for travelers. Luck of the dice can also be too decisive in bad places. I'd say this game has prioritized randomness and replayability before strategy and gameplay. That said, it's rather fun to play, if you accept and adapt your mind to the luck element. The game also looks nice and is rarely totally samey.

Fun factor: 4/5
Strategy vs. luck: 2/5
Mechanics/gameplay tied to the theme: 2/5 (voyages where travel isn't the most important aspect...)
Component/art quality: 4/5
Replayability: 4/5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The dice rolling and spending and bidding for actions is an interesting mechanic, and the result of the goodness of it. This game gets its score solely from this mechanic, as every other element is really bad.

Everything else has from moderate to heavy faults, though. The characters are not exactly balanced and will need experienced players to know how to balance each other out. The cities have some variance to them but traveling is not that rewarding, and contracts are a mix of costly and efficient contracts, leaving you to luck in the end. Is that newbie player countering the others appropriately? Are they even helping another unknowingly? Why do i get contracts costing too much gold, needing to make an inefficient one dice for one contract replacement or a dice efficient one dice for two contracts that will be luck dependent if i wait that long?

And that is how my first play went. Four player game, one getting resources for free when others gathered resources. I had an extra contract and dice per turn, but the worst hidden objective combination ever: literally go to the farthest northern, southern, eastern and western cities. The other two players had powers inherently benefiting from travel: 0 gold dice placing and another placed houses wherever they passed. Yet, they spent all their time doing resources and doing contracts instead of traveling a lot like they should have. I got gold-heavy contracts for little points that were unfeasible to change, leaving me to spend all my dice to get gold, and give gold to my opponent. That player ended up buying contracts the whole game and just doing them for free, winning me in contracts for 2 or 3 extra and 10 total. I traveled strategically, picking very good advantages, but could only get in the end 1 point for my hidden objectives.

So those are my biggest complaints: The map is badly designed, comboing your hidden objectives is really bad since there is an extremely high variance in difficulty. The player who could drop houses wherever they passed had the tightest route, that was around 7 moves while i had the longest one with around 16 moves, mostly expensive ones, or more if i wanted cheaper moves (but would end up costing more gold instead of more resources). The third is the variable power design. It looks like it is good, but in practice it is bad when combined with the map and objectives.

RATING: 8.3
Took four games to get past the feeling that I barely knew what I was doing. It's [i]hard[/i] and mind-bending far beyond what the game looks like (echoes of Agricola there). All actions are expensive, you never have enough of any resource, and at the end of a turn, you frequently have left-over dice you don't quite know what to do with. Starting to feel very well put together, and invites playing again to try and figure it out more. Speeds up a lot after the first few plays.
I suspect I'm going to be extremely impressed at how well balanced the different characters (with asymmetrical abilities) are. Lovely artwork and tactile pieces. Lots of attention to detail.

Can be extremely frustrating, though, when things just don't come together, while players with a good start will seem to snowball ahead.

RATING: 8
Az utóbbi évek egyik legjobb eurogém-e. Valamiért a Kingsburg jutott eszembe róla, de annál sokkal de sokkal mélyebb, változatosabb és mindenekelőtt jobb.
Euro-hoz képest egész sok kütyü van benne, a setup se 2 perc, de ez a setup jó arra, hogy ne legyen 2 egyforma parti. A térképen szereplő városok jellemzőit random kártyákkal határozzuk meg, és minden játékos random szerepet kap, és ezek a szerepek adják a játék sava-borsát. Az alapszabályok is nagyon jól egybe vannak gyúrva, de ezek a szerepek még adnak az egésznek egy erős csavart. Az egyik, ami nagyon tetszett az a kockák egymásra helyezése. A másik ami jó, hogy a térkép tematikus. A Himaláya az Himaláya, azt nem lehet csak úgy kerülgetni. Ha valaki elindul egyik úton, az elég komolyan elkötelezi magát, és mivel összesen 4 vagy 5 teljesen különálló út van, legalább ennyi teljesen különálló kombó kínálkozik a városok tulajdonságaiból. Persze van olyan szerep vagy olyan kártyák, amik segítségével akár egész nagy része is beutazható a térképnek, illetve nem is feltétlenül kell erőltetni az utazást, lehet hogy valakinek a szerződések teljesítése szimpatikusabb. Ha nem lenne elég a városokban rejlő sok lehetőség, vannak beépített akciók is. Ideiglenesen szerezhetők újabb kockák, amik nagyon megnövelik a lehetőségeinket. A játék a sokrétűsége ellenére nagyon koherens, nem nagyon tudnék belekötni. Talán egyedül a célkártyákat érzem kicsit feleslegesnek, és a szerepek kiegyensúlyozottsága kérdéses, de szerintem mindegyikkel lehet nyerni. A játék a bonyolultságához képest egész rövid, 2 óra kb. Ajánlom mindenkinek, aki a kockás eurokat szereti.

RATING: 8
Hans im Glück mid-weight + the (Italian duo of) designers of Tzol'kin + artwork by Michael Menzel = a game I'm interested in!

Marco Polo is not an innovative game: everything was already seen elsewhere. But in this game, the mechanics conjure to create a fresh and entertaining play experience: people at Hans im Glück are masters of development, and here again they struck a perfect balance.

There is much going on: dice as workers, route building, characters with awesome powers, insane amounts of variable set-up, additional actions... but everything plays smoothly, and after the initial explanation, the five rounds of talks he game fly and everything fits nicely.

It scales decently to 3 and 2 (but don't use Raschid with 2: it removes a big part of the game with just 2...), and I am eager to play again!
The initial rating (8) could possibly go up!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good game. It makes dice placement its core mechanism, but doesn't overly rely on rolling 5 or 6s. In fact, sometimes it greatly benefits you to roll lower numbers. The designers did such a good job of making the dice rolling feel completely balanced that, if it wasn't for the stacking penalty, I would be inclined to argue that this game would be improved by removing the dice and moving more towards a conventional worker placement. 

Each game of Marco Polo presents you with a randomized board state. To play well, you must process that board state into a workable strategy. For example, in Marco Polo, you first analyze the randomized actions and bonuses present on the board, decide what type of strategy is likely to work best in this situation, and select an available character tile. Due to the limited number of rounds in the game, you need to be disciplined in ignoring certain parts of the board that are unfit for your strategy. Does the board state and your character power diminish the importance of traveling? If so, you should consider focusing on contracts and being careful to not waste actions on unneeded travel. Does the board state and character power increase the importance of traveling? If so, you should consider focusing on grabbing bonuses early and/or racing to Beijing or Sumatra. 

Those decisions, however, have the potential for the game to fall flat for some people. For example, choosing the one-time bonuses and unlocking city-specific actions are fun. But, due to the inherent amount of randomization and the vastly different player powers, some games of Marco Polo may require a player or two to ignore those interesting aspects. This can leave the game feeling a bit dry and boring for those players.

Marco Polo is a well made game of familiar mechanisms that, despite not bringing anything amazingly novel, still feels quite interesting. But it seems to lack a hook that makes me want to play it as much as other games that I rate as high or higher. For dice-based action selection, I still find Troyes to be superior. For asymmetric player powers combined with grand strategy, I find Terra Mystica to be superior. However, Marco Polo plays better at 2 than Terra Mystica.

With so many different 'overpowered' characters to choose from and a different map set-up each time, there isn't a sure win strategy - players would have to adapt differently to the game everytime.

RATING: 9.5
I enjoy the theme of this game. The graphic design is gorgeous with vibrant colors. The components and mechanisms are well done. This is one of the best dice placement games available. The asymmetrical powers really get your attention in the game. You look at them and think several are broken, but I haven't found that to be the case. Their powers seem to balance out mostly. There is enough randomness to the setup to keep re-playability high. An experienced player benefits from knowing how to balance travel and completing contracts. Travel is very difficult to pull off for the inexperienced. Some characters just don't do well with it. Also, the state of the opening board makes a big difference.

RATING: 7.6
Euro clásico de colocación de trabajadores, recolección de recursos y demás. Tenemos un peón, con el que debemos ir viajando por ciertos emplazamientos del tablero, con la idea de cumplir unos objetivos que se han repartido al inicio del juego. Además, el primero en llegar a una ubicación, recibe un bonus. Todo el que llega a una ubicación construye allí un puesto comercial que le habilita para disponer de una nueva acción.
Está interesante el tema de los recursos. LAs acciones se realizan con dados, en función de su valor tendrán un alcance u otro. Si utilizas una acción que ya ha sido usada por un jugador tiene un coste adicional.
También existe una accion de obtener contratos, que cumples entregando unas mercancías y obteniendo una recompensa.

El juego es  bastante sencillo, pero da para pensar un buen rato. Hay una serie de personajes que se reparten al inicio y que le dan una caracteristica propia a cada jugador. En mi opinión, el personaje del mercader está desproporcionado. Es demasiado potente.

Está interesante.

RATING: 8
Using rolled dice as worker placement. Asymmetric power. Variable set-up.

Scales well at 2,3,4 players. 

Front heavy decision making ala Terra Mystica. This has echoes of being Terra Mystica-lite. However, the imbalance of the characters seem to be much greater than TM.

Fast play time - about 60 to 90 minutes.

The luck factor from the dice generally doesn't lose you a game - but a string of bad rolls can set you back. 

3 games in: filling contracts seems to be a lot easier than travelling.

8 games in: traveling can be done well - just harder!

Update Dec 2017. Dropped from 8.5 to 8. Some seriously broken characters. No pretensions or semblance of any balance. In fact, the game seems to thrive on the outrageousness of the imbalanced characters.

RATING: 8
Very entertaining dice-placement Euro. I like the "diminishing returns" mechanic in which the first die placed either sets the minimum requirement for future dice, or requires players to pay more when they place. The contracts give short-term goals while the route bonuses give long-term ones. 

The special powers are interesting and all very good, though we've found that a couple of the characters are definitively worse than the others, especially in a two-player game (Kublai Khan, for example, is near-worthless with 2-p. when competition for precedence in routes is much less important).

RATING: 9
This had gotten some so-so press in the circles I travel in, plus the dice-as-workers mechanic is about my least favorite ever. So I had kinda kept my distance. But once I played it, I quite enjoyed it. The special powers of the characters and the varying city activation abilities are what make the game; otherwise there isn't a lot here, I'd say. But the various powers are cool - almost an Argent: The Consortium kind of vibe. Also nice is that at 5 turns, it's pretty brisk and doesn't overstay its welcome. Anyway, I'll probably end up picking this up, although similar to Russian Rails I imagine the life expectancy will be a bit short. Hans im Glück has also lost their edge in terms of isolating balance problems, so I wouldn't be super-surprised to find out there aren't that many viable strategies (Russian Rails had that problem). Although, I should say that I've won every game I've played with a different character card each time; everyone I play with always thinks there is one character who is overpowered, but I sincerely doubt this is true (although I do think a couple of the "advanced" ones need favorable board layouts). I do think, though, that the route to winning has to go through fulfilling a lot of contracts. Another concern is that with a lot of high-stakes decisions being made in the initial round (character choice and strategic route choice) and with a lot of up-front complexity, the game is probably a bit front-loaded and lacking in narrative pulse (the game's tension also fails to build to a climax at the end, instead sort of petering out). But that's all the difference between the good and the great; Marco Polo seems pretty good and I'll enjoy playing it some more.

RATING: 6
Voyages of Marco Polo was interesting to check out for a play, but I'm not sure there's much there that I'd personally want to revisit. An interesting puzzle to roll your dice, and then try to decide how to allocate those dice to various actions.

In general, you'll use your dice to gain resources and money, which you'll use to travel around the board to place markers in various cities, which will give you access to more special actions. You'll also be fulfilling goal tiles with your resources, and some hidden goals for visiting certain cities.

Overall, a neat puzzle, but nothing that really stands out from the crowd for me, or that I feel would be interesting to explore repeatedly. I'd be up for it if someone else suggested it though.

RATING: 7.5
I like Tzolk’in quite a bit, although I don’t own it anymore (I sold it as my brother owns it too). So I was intrigued when I got to hear a lot of high praise about this game. A clever use of dice in a somewhat heavy strategy game, lasting 45-60 minutes by the designers of Tzolk’in? Yes please! However, I’m not absolutely convinced that it’s as good as Tzolk’in.

The asymetric characters are great, although I fear that they might dictate your strategy a little too much… Assymetric abilities are generally something I appreciate a lot; it works splendid in Terra Mystica. Here, I do think that the abilities dictate a bit too much of your strategy which is something I don’t find in TM; sure, you are probably leaning in some directions in TM and other games, but when you get a guy like, for instance Marco+Niccolo Polo, you’re kind of forced to travel if you want to succeed. And with one of the other characters (forgot his name), you’re forced to do that too as you get extra points for placing your extra settlements. On the other hand, without these special character abilities, the game would be boring and lack variety.
Speaking of traveling (admittedly, after only a couple of plays), I find travelling to be too expensive and not worth the effort. Sure, you do need to get into a couple of cities to activate the special abilities, and if you get there first you get a bonus, but I find it expensive to try to travel a lot - fulfilling contracts seems easier and has a better reward. But again, future plays will reveal much more of the deeper strategy (and these further plays seem to have proved that the game lacks longevity), and again, your character kind of determines your strategy…

RATING: 4
Despite the game's simplicity I find it very difficult to state with certainty wether the designers and developers have done a good job of it. It is about worker placement, but instead of using actual meeple you use dice. The dice in most cases indicate how often a selected action can be taken; but there are also a few spots where it determines the selection range. It is then a matter of rolling your dice, and when it is your turn place one or a few on the board and immediately take the associated action. If you want to use an action already claimed, you have to pay money. It's a rythmical variation on the usual worker placement mechanism and as such very easy to get into. So what are the actions used for: obtaining resources or contracts mostly, and then spending those resources on the contracts to obtain money and VP. You can also opt to travel along a stylised map of Asia and put up trading houses in various cities; this gives you the ability to take the action associated with that city by placing a die there. These actions may or may not be worth it. And sic transit gloria mundi.

Despite all this which is admittedly very enthusiast-family-friendly, I [i]really[/i] have very strong doubts about the game's balance owing to its asymmetry. Asymmetry is really a can of worms, both on the players' as well as the designers' behalf, so I have to be careful. But still, in the game I played using the basic characters the player who got a specific but free resource every time someone used the market utterly [i]dominated[/i] the entire game by fulfilling contract after contract to the point where it became embarassing. Now I was spending resources on actions which didn't deliver, so obviously I had it coming and then some... but when I turned the question around, as in 'Okay, what should I have done?' I came up completely empty-handed. The layout of the board was such that only one other approximately competitive path existed (simply look at what would give VPs and work out a little engine around it), and that depended heavily on being the only person to be able to use it, and not be burdened by money issues (which happened to be the case). The moment there would be competition for that path it would've lost its power. The game was seemingly lost before it began, but it took us 90 minutes to find out.

Again, asymmetries are a minefield, and because of the randomised cities layout this horrible experience could very well have been a fluke. But it leaves a very sour aftertaste in my mouth that at least one character doesn't really need to use the travel-part at all, and can ignore the important market most of the time too. (Apart from that, travel is an expensive hobby, and the bonuses for putting up lots of trading houses are very, very small. For a game which is about Marco Polo having to fight the game to travel for a bit is a big turn-off, to be honest.) I therefore fear it is the dreaded situation that although the game may eventually turn out to be sufficiently well-balanced, this particular path is simply easy to play [i]very well[/i]. In other words it is balanced VP-wise, but not skill-wise. And that is neither fun nor good design at all.

Until the inevitable discussions about the characters' perceived and actual power appear, complete with insights in how to properly play what appear to be the weak characters, I'm going to put in a low rate. For the moment I have no interest in playing again and up my skills the hard way.

[b]Update[/b] Played anew, with the character strong in journeying, got some decent traction in that I finished a clear 2nd... and got lapped within 10 VP by the character who owned the merchant. Riiiiiight.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is great mix of different mechanics brought together in one engaging euro game. This is not a light game, it needs some practice to grasp everything and play efficiently according to the circumstances, but it definitely works. It is mostly about finding the most optimal route how you should play according to your character and different game setup.

What I like:
- very engaging mix of mechanics tied together very well (dice placement, source gathering, point to point movement, character drafting, worker placement, variable player powers). The game doesn't introduce anything new (although a lot of it was new for me) but everything works very smoothly
- I have to stress that I love various power of the characters. Each of them has powerful abilities yet still they are (mostly) comparable in strength. Some of them focus on movement, some on resources (and therefore contracts) but all of them play very differently
- The setup is very variable from game to game which means every game is different and you should apply your strategy accordingly. Sometimes there are a lot of money available so you can move a lot, sometimes there are no money but lot of resources - let's prefer contracts. 

What I dislike:
- The difference between small and big (5x) resources is not very big. You have to check the resource carefully to differentiate between two sizes - the solution could be to mark bigger ones somehow, but we simply play as all of them are the same (there is enough resources for two player game)
- Sometimes (mostly when you are behind and cannot do much) the game feels like it ends before it started. Five rounds runs very quickly sometimes. But for this reason the games are tight and you are always under pressure of time (you should manage your resources carefully to do the best from it).
- The setup and tear down is longish comparing to the length of the game (speaking mostly about 2 player games). You need to play at least two games in a row to be worth the effort.
- I somehow feel that the north route is the safest and strongest among the four ways to Peking. This is different in more player games as there are more blocking, but in 2 player games I see a lot travel tickets use cities from the north. I use this route most of the times and I would prefer to have more variety (but I don't know how much it is just my personal preference and how much it this opinion objective)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Secret Santa 2015

It's funny, but this is my "train game."  Wait, what?

Think about it - you're building routes between cities, getting resources and powers from those cities, and using them to activate facilities at other cities.  You need to connect cities, collect resources, build facilities, etc.  It's all train stuff, just with a different theme (with zero actual trains).

It seems to me that train games are basically stock/business games, with a little bit of route building.  I like the route building, but not the business part.  MP gives me all of that.

The special powers are cool, and the variable setup (power, route goals, city bonuses, etc.) leads to very different puzzles to solve each game.  Love that - that's our favourite thing about games.  Every game gives you the same basic framework, but you need to react to the setup at the start, and the die rolls each turn.  And just like other dice games we like (e.g. Castles of Burgundy) there is *just enough* luck mitigation to prevent frustration but avoid triviality.

RATING: 10
2-4

Setup : zie spelregels

Verloop van een ronde :

1/ Nieuwe startspeler bepalen (vervalt in de eerste ronde):
De speler die in de vorige ronde als laatste de actie “reizen” heeft gekozen, wordt de nieuwe startspeler. Hij neemt de zandloper. Geen van beide de actie “reizen” uitgevoerd, dan blijft de huidige startspeler in functie.

2/ Stads- en karakterbonussen uitkeren:
2a. alle kleine steden (met een 1 gemarkeerd) keren nu hun bonussen uit. Iedere speler krijgt van elke kleine stad waar hij een handelspost heeft de betreffende bonus.
2b. alle karakters die met een ! zijn gemarkeerd, keren nu hun bonussen uit.

3/ Dobbelstenen terugnemen:
Iedereen neemt zijn kleur dobbelstenen terug. De zwarte dobbelstenen gaan terug naar hun locatie op het bord.

4/ Dobbelstenen werpen, eventueel compensatie:
Wanneer een speler minder dan 15 ogen heeft gegooid, dan krijgt hij een compensatie in geld en/of kamelen.

5/ Elke speler is afwisselend aan de beurt. Dit gaat door tot geen enkele speler nog dobbelstenen heeft.

Verloop van een beurt:

A De speler voert zoveel extra acties uit als hij kan en wil.
B De speler moet 1 actie uitvoeren.
C De speler voert zoveel extra acties uit als hij kan en wil.

RATING: 8
[2015-05-26 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
The Voyages of Marco Polo (VoMP) reminds of Terra Mystica in that you have to make a lot of decisions before the game has even started. Because of the variable setup, there are better and worse paths and characters to choose. Additionally, there doesn't seem to be alot of room for mistakes. Don't play your best game from the beginning and you're out of the race for the win against competent opponents. I've seen this in all of my games so far and it has soured some of my gaming buddies who don't want to play VoMP again. There simple isn't any catch-up mechanism and it's very easy to screw up.

Although VoMP is a unforgiving game and I've watched my gaming buddies run away with the win, I still enjoy it quite a lot. Whenever I lost badly, I could always tell which of my decisions had cost me the game.

RATING: 9
Players are medieval merchants, travelling and exploiting the markets of the East, by trading and fulfilling contracts.  Lovely looking worker/dice placement game, that uses dice to place actions that gain goods, trigger movement across a map that spans from Italy to Asia, gain contracts, and so on.  It's familiar stuff that feels a bit Stone Age (possibly...) but with a wide open game space to explore, and not enough time in a single game to try it all out.  Hans im Glück tend to be my go to publisher these days, as they seem to consistently maintain churn out properly developed games - and they haven't disappointed here.  Like this one a lot.

RATING: 8
Fun dice placement game, with nice components - actually there is a generous amount of stuff in the box (game is language independent), medium complexity, with swift playtime - in fact you can play this in about 45 minutes, though set up is above average.
Dice enhance what is really a resource management game, with large dose of travelling around a very camel coloured board. Which all sounds a bit bland, but the addition of the special player powers greatly enhances the replayability, which is quite obviously very high, and variability, which was already quite high with variable goal cards - like tickets in TtR, which also increase replayability. I did get the feeling that some characters were 'over powered' to start with, but with repeated plays I have come to realise that all the characters are good and I just very silly - though I always feel like my opponents powers are better than mine when I feel like - or actually am as if often the case- losing, usually very badly, even though I chose mine and shed tears to get it. Whoops. 
So you've got a delightful, almost family weight - depending on your family - medium weight euro with nice bits, variability, and who doesn't like rolling dice? Nothing really that innovative; my only problem with it is that I quite like it, rather than being ga ga over it.

RATING: 7
A very solid and enjoyable mish-mash of dice allocation, resource set collection, and network connections.   Perhaps the signature mechanism is that the lowest value of a group of dice determines the strength of an action and also the cost if you are not first in that action space.   Money is tight and map movement is therefore more difficult than it might seem.   

The small vs large bits (1s vs 5s) are a little hard to distinguish, but other than that the production is nice and pops in a very polished, if familiar, way.   The special abilities and variable setup should help with some replayability, but I wouldn't expect to get 20+ plays out of this, realistically.   Still, it is an enjoyable game and one that I would consider adding to my collection perhaps.

RATING: 8.5
Marco Polo is brilliant.  Think Feld's Bora Bora, think Stone Age, think Ticket to Ride, think Village, think Alien Frontiers, think them all then think again.  This game takes bits of many many things and creates something very unique and very special indeed.  Freshness yet familiarity, which are not natural bed fellows, but when you suceed in combining them you have a winner.  This is something bigger than the sum of its parts, something that brings a smile to your face just to think about it.

The game itself is very very good.  What sets it apart and creates something brilliant are the character powers.  Each player has a power which isn't just a minor improvement but a wildly powerful improvement.  Think the differences in the Terra Mystica races, then add some more power asymmetry.  Of course we rely on the game having been sufficiently play-tested to create equality in these huge differences, but this is a 
Hans Im Gluck game so we should have confidence.


This is my game of the year so far, and better than any new heavy Euro I played from 2014, and I doubt anything will come close in 2015, even with Essen around the corner.  Where will it end up after some time?  After one play it is tough to say, but whilst it might not reach the giddy heights of my top heavy Euros (Terra Mystica, Agricola, World Without End), it should settle to be the equal of the ones I personally rank just below the untouchables (eg. Bora Bora, Hansa Teutonica, Lancaster, Ora & Labora, Navegador, Goa...).

Deep joy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Marco Polo is an excellent dice-based worker placement game. There are a fair number of these now: Alien Frontiers, Kingsburg, Stone Age, etc. Marco Polo is a heavier, more thought-provoking game than all of the previously mentioned titles. Beyond that, Marco Polo is unique in the fact that it has powerful ameritrash-levels of asymmetrical player powers. For example, there is a character that doesn't roll his dice... he places them on whatever side he wants. 

Players are mainly trying to collect resources to fulfill contracts and get money to travel to the cities on the map. The player power you get will help determine your focus or balance. The game has an extreme levels of variability due to the player powers, the randomized action spaces/incomes on the cities, contracts, and hidden city objective cards. The game is not perfect however. There are some small fiddly rules that are easy to mess up and different areas of the board have different dice placement rules. If you're willing to put in the effort though, this is a wonderful game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Nice workerplacement, but way too much downtime with the wrong people at the table, and not much interaction with the other players

RATING: 10
This game is fantastic. The five rounds pass quickly, but the game always seems to end right at its peak; a characteristic I greatly appreciate. The player powers are incredibly strong, but seem to be at least moderately balanced, and they inject loads of replayability into the game, as do the various city actions, and town bonuses. The theme may not be very strong, but the mechanics are surprisingly elegant. Actions are never blocked, but you'll have to shell out big money if you keep taking actions after your opponents. Strategies are plentiful and balanced, and there is always something new to be learned. If find myself thinking about this game long after each game session, and am perpetually awaiting my next play.

RATING: 8
(Rating based on 80+ asynch online plays on Yucata.de [microbadge=19345])

You do have to play a few games so you get the hang of it. There are multiple goals you try to accomplish and the timing of when you do what is crucial.

A big plus is certainly the different player powers. Figuring out which one fits your play style the best is pretty cool.

The game is not without its flaws. For instance, traveling to certain cities gives you access to additional spots to place your workers. But very often, I found that those spots are not even worth the hassle.

Overall, an enjoyable experience.

RATING: 9.4
Voyages is another game that restricts players' actions throughout the entirety of the game. Players only roll 5 dice per round (there are exceptions) and must use those dice to activate areas of the board that allow travel and resource gathering to fill contracts. Sometimes high valued dice will be wasted because everything is based on the lowest valued dice. Sometimes a player may only have 2 actions over the entire round while another player may have 5. It's a fast paced game that allows for a lot of depth and stressful decisions.

RATING: 6
There are many things I like about this game. I like the mix of fortune and strategy, and particularly the fact that the former doesn't dominate by any means. I like the character powers, which lead to different playstiles. I love the components. 

Why, then, do I give it only a 6? Two reasons: first, the rulebook could have been organized A LOT better. It's sometimes hard to find important information if you can't remember it. Second, and perhaps more important: the travel mechanic, that I feel should be central in a game about a famous historical traveler, is underwhelming. If you don't have a character who STRONGLY encourages you to travel, doing so is quite impractical: it costs you two dice every time, and it has the potential to cost you a ton of money and a ton of camels. In all the three games I played, the winner was the player who stayed in Venice or close to Venice and spent all their actions to fullfill contracts. Objectives are worth a ridiculously low amount of points, not enough to justify investing towards reaching them, and the town bonuses might or might not be worth it, given that they are placed randomly on the map. The end result if that you don't use a core game mechanic and a good half of the game board most of the time, which is just sad.

RATING: 6
It was good.  It ran a little long with 4p and 3 newbies, I think it would be better with 3.  The dice were interesting; in general, you claim an action by putting a die/dice in a row; some actions require 1, some require 2-3 dice.  When it requires more than one die, the column you pick is dictated by the *lowest* die in your group.  Also, if somebody is already in a row, you can still place but you must pay money equal to your lowest die first.  You can then take the action dictated by that column, or a lower column if you want.
So higher dice aren’t always better; they’re more expensive to use in some cases, so the order you pair up your dice becomes critical.

RATING: 9
2017-08-09:
This still stands as my best game of 2015.
I really like how the characters each individually seems totally broken, but since we all got a 'broken' ability it evens out. :D I also like how much randomness you can get with the setup. And I like how the subtle interaction really can screw with you and you have to think about when to actually do the travel if you want to be first player.

Some minor dislikes is how horribly the randomness of the expansion tiles can strike for someone who completes a contract with them. A tile with '1 point for each completed contract' just at the start of the game is sooooo much worse than getting a free movement for instance. I also am a bit scared about how swingy the starting 'ticket'-cards can be if someone is able to get two cards that really suit together AND also has a random setup that gives a good combo on the city-cards. I also dislike how important contracts are, but that is just my taste since I find them boring in contrast to traveling.
But. . those are still minor complaints. I REALLY like this game.

Current rating: 9 (leaning a bit towards 8, but this has held up for quite a few plays and deserves a 9)

RATING: 8
DEC 2017: If you've played the other "Italian dice euros" (Grand Austria Hotel, Lorenzo il Magnifico), then you know what to expect. A more systems-focused euro that has scarcity at its core as players try to complete trade contracts, travel for markets, and complete trade routes. A great thinky euro for those who love this sort of thing, and the setting of Marco Polo's travels is a nice change-up. One of those 10-plays-and-done puzzly euros: for my tastes, once the puzzle has be played a few times, I lose interest. I prefer more combo-heavy, more variable games such as Grand Austria Hotel.

I predict 10 or less plays, and the rating to float from 8 to 6 over those 10 plays. Just not enough of what I want from a game.

RATING: 7
Kicsit vegyesfelvágott euro, amiben azért elég sok játéklehetőség rejtőzik. Nem voltam vele 100%-osan elégedett, de mégis rögtön a következő partyn, sőt partykon törtem a fejem. Szóval azért olyan rossz mégse lehetett.
Nagy újrajátszhatóságot adnak a karakterek, és a mindig változó tábla, ami szerintem jó eséllyel alapjaiban változtatja meg a nyerő stratégiát, ha már van némi rutin ahhoz, hogy ezt át is lássuk. :)
A kockalehelyezős részben volt némi csalódás, mert azért simán lehet rosszat dobni. Annak ellenére, hogy a kis kockákat is fel lehet használni itt-ott, Azért összességében sokkal jobb a nagy dobás. A kockaforgatós képességeket meg túl drágának éreztem a hasznosságukhoz képest. Logikusan 5-6-ért senki nem fog újradobást reszkírozni, mert semmi értelme. Az egyet hozzáadás, pedig két tevéért azért drága. A plusz kocka vétel esetleg még a reális opció, bár 3 tevéért az se olcsó. Jó lett volna több machinálási lehetőség, kicsivel olcsóbban, hogy kevésbé számítson a dobás maga.
Fura volt kicsit a térkép, nomeg a ezzel együtt a hálózatépítéses célok is. Nem értem miért kellett gyakorlatilag 4-5 teljesen különálló útvonalra bontani a térképet. Olyan kevés a lépés akció a játékban, hogy ha elindulsz az egyiken, akkor esélyed sincs már átmenni a másikra. Ez viszont kellemetlen a céloknál, illetve ha valaki elindul a nyakadba ugyanazon az útvonalon, amin te, akkor logikusan sokkal nehezebb játékod lesz, mint annak, aki egyedül császkál végig egy úton.
Node kell még játszani vele mindenképp. :)
Egyelőre egy vegyes hetes.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The Voyages of Marco Polo is another variation on the worker-/dice-placement mechanism and as such only offers a few small tweaks to games you have seen elsewhere before. It is probably a little more variable (thanks to the setup) and a little less interactive (thanks to... well... almost no interaction; even the typical blocking of action spaces is heavily nerfed) than most but if there is a property that sets it apart from most competitors it's that the selection of a character at the beginning of the game strongly guides your strategy right from the start.

The relative merits and strengths of those characters have already been discussed quite a bit and some say that since different people consider different characters to be vastly overpowered, the game should be fairly balanced after all. And they might well be right. At this point I'm at least cautiously optimistic. I do still consider it a possibility that different characters are vastly overpowered depending on the random starting setup, however, and that would mean that it just takes you 60 to 90 minutes just to see who took the most valuable character at the start. That would be a pity!

RATING: 8
I must say that given the familiar mechanics of a dice game, Marco Polo has a great deal of breadth and depth that really help make it feel singular among all of the games of this type.  Lots of interesting decisions to make, very easy to understand and yet lots of nuanced things that you won't truly understand until multiple plays. Tons of replayability with modular action spaces. Presumably a very tight game as well, and amazingly well-balanced considering how different the roles and actions can be. A very interesting game and very well constructed. Can be a little AP and there are times where you are finished your turn before everyone else, so you just sit around for awhile waiting for people to finish. Also, some parts of the game (like the contracts) feel so Euro-y in terms of "trade this-for-that" that it can take you out of the theme. Those issues aside, two strong thumbs up.

RATING: 8.5
Marco Polo scales very well, two player games gallop along to a conclusion within 45 minutes. It is extremely satisfying to discover a clever synergy between you character, the city abilities and your end game city scoring cards. I generally like strategic dice rolling games and Marco Polo has just the right balance of actions available to let you progress your plans, but still end up wishing that you had another action or two. It is not as complex as Castle of Burgundy, but gives you more to think about then Kingsburg. I would suggest that you put any initial concerns regarding balance on the back burner and play the game a few times to explore its possibilities. Hopefully, you will come around to appreciating the bold decisions that the designers have made.

RATING: 7
A German puzzle Euro that focuses on a nice mix of tactical and strategic decision, Voyages of Marco Polo is an action selection game via dice. Throughout the game, you travel with a focus on set collection to complete contracts and managing resources for future planning. The planning is forgiving, as most choices only need to be thought out  one or two turns in advance. Many games do the same things as Voyages, but Voyages of Marco Polo is (mostly) pleasant to play and friendly in its willingness to let players plan their next move. At times, it can be stressful trying to achieve plans, but when successful, makes them that much more rewarding.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
A Selyemút nyomán
A játékban kalandor-kereskedőink (Marco Polo és barátai) az Európától keletre, délkeletre eső területek bejárásával és közben előnyös kereskedelmi szerződések megkötésével igyekeznek minél több győzelmi pontot megszerezni. Több út is vezet a sikerhez: bizonyos helyszínek meglátogatása, a helyszínek elsőnek történő felfedezése és az előjogok begyűjtése, Pekingbe történő megérkezés, kereskedelmi szerződések teljesítése, kereskedőállomások létesítése, a már létesített állomások képességeinek kihasználása (illetve ezek megfelelő kombinálása). Mindez nyakon öntve tevékkel, árucikkekkel és kockadobással. A filmek kapcsán beigazolódott mondás itt is igaz: „mindenki nyúl le ötleteket, de valaki ezzel sem tud mit kezdeni”. Sok más játékban már megismert játékmechanika köszön vissza ránk egy nagyon kellemes elegyben. Kis kockadobálás, munkáslehelyezés, a helyek korlátozott száma, árúk gyűjtögetése, majd célok (itt szerződések) teljesítése, …
A rengeteg lehetőség (és annak játék eleji megfelelő kiértékelése) miatt inkább már haladó társasozóknak ajánlanám, de nekik mindenképpen egy próbát megér.
A sok pozitív élmény mellett játék legnagyobb negatívuma számomra a benne játszható karakterek kiegyensúlyozatlansága, amely játékosszámtól függően is változhat.

Dice Rolling
Route/Network Building
Variable Player Powers
Dice-Worker Placement

>> Variable setup and player powers and rolling dice as your action pool means no two games play the same. Filled with agonizing and interesting decisions, beautiful to look at, and an amazing experience for a little over an hour playtime makes this the perfect game for me.

It is not really  a strategic game. It is more a tactcal one but  it is a lot of luck and radomness in here. Somtimes if your dice result is really poor it is not even possible to make a good move. You higly depend on your dice and the moves of your opponents even your special power could. 
And at the end it's not really innovative. One advantage is that the game doesn't last too long. (2-3 Hours with 4 players)

RATING: 10
An elegant example of how to use dice typical 6-sided in a board game in that the dice are not opponents to the players. Dice isn't used as a Pass/Fail mechanic but rather as a random element that encourages interesting choices. Neither a higher value nor a lower value is better nor worse. Just depends on the circumstances. 

The dice just lifts the worker placement mechanic to a higher level which is beautiful to see. Players don't need to "hope" for a certain dice result. Everyone just deals with their worker dices while considering their opponents. Player therefore do not "fight" against the dice. Unlike something such as Stone Age where the player "hope" for dice results and groan when they roll poorly.

RATING: 8
規則:
*洗勻18張目標卡，6小城市版塊按字母放置，大城市版塊隨機放7個，剩下的不用，大城市卡隨機放9張，剩下的不用，起始契約卡放旁邊，然後抽30張形成五疊，一疊六張，剩下的蓋著放旁邊成為特殊契約區，第一疊開在契約區，五顆黑骰放在市集區上方，跑最多國家的起始
*每個玩家拿對應的顏色五顆骰子，房子放在個人圖版上，拿一個起始契約，起始兩個駱駝，金錢照順位增加1，首位7元，首次玩建議角色，兩個人一個放分數，一個放起始地點，每個玩家抽兩張目標卡
*遊戲進行五輪，每輪分成6個階段，之後最後記分
1.決定起始玩家 由最後進行旅行的玩家成為此回合的起始玩家，首輪隨便，沒人的話順位不變
2.小城市跟角色收入
3.玩家把骰子拿回手上
4.玩家擲骰並放在圖版上(總和小於15，差值可拿對應的金錢或駱駝，可隨意搭配)
5.輪流進行回合，每回合必須做一次強制動作，前後可進行額外動作
***強制動作 放骰子進行動作，每一個動作每輪自己顏色的骰子只能出現一次，一放就執行該動作，骰面最小的骰子決定可執行幾次或是拿對應資源，行動可少作，但至少一次
A不管骰面都拿五元
B市集 放對應顆數骰子，按骰面最小的拿對應欄資源，可拿同行較少的那欄
C漢賞賜 放的骰子要大於等於前面的，拿資源三選一跟兩駱駝
D獲得契約 選小於骰面的1~2個契約，如果自己的擺滿可棄到特殊契約牌庫底
*契約拿完往左推齊，如果全部抽完從特殊契約牌庫抽兩張補
E旅行 放兩顆骰跟舉骰面小的 決定最多可移動的步數，付錢，根據移動過的地點付駱駝或錢，停在城市就自動蓋交易站(一個城市最多一個人一個)
*有交易站的大城市，該行動你可用第一個蓋的還可拿額外好處，小城市直接獲得上面收入，且每回合可在拿，移動方向沒有限制，如果全放完又需要放交易站則改為移動交易站
*大城市行動一輪只能一個人使用，轉換率看骰點
***額外動作***
A完成契約 獲得獎勵並蓋著在遊戲最後比數目
B一顆骰換三元
C一駱駝重骰一顆骰
D兩駱駝 骰點+-1，6跟1不能互換
E三駱駝獲得一顆黑骰並骰跟之後可放，一回合只能做一次
*如果要使用別人使用過的行動，支付骰點數較小的那顆骰對應金錢，就可把骰放在別人骰上使用該行動
6.輪結束，清空契約，換新的
*最後記分，平手比駱駝數
1.目標卡記分，每張目標卡如果上面兩個地點都完成就有分，下面則是指兩張目標卡上不同地點到過幾個，或得分數
2.10元一分
3.到北京的拿北京分，且可兩資源轉一分，駱駝不能轉
4.完成最多契約的加七分
**2-3人
3人 黑骰四顆 漢賞賜格放一顆點數一的中立骰
2人 黑骰三顆 漢賞賜格放兩顆點數一的中立骰，一顆中立骰放在拿五元的位置
**進階規則***
*小城鎮版塊隨機放
*洗勻角色卡，開玩家數加一張，尾家開始選角色
*目標卡抽四選二
**角色卡
1.可指定骰點
2.每輪開始多一白骰跟一個契約(從特殊契約抽)
3.當有人拿市場 或漢賞賜 或五元 可獲得好處
4.北京開始
5.兩個人 每輪開始一駱駝
6.放有人放的動作不用付錢
7.起始多兩棟交易所，經過城鎮就可以蓋，11棟蓋完加10分
8.每輪收入三元，可花一個移動任意在綠洲間移動

RATING: 7
A game about ruthless action efficiency. It's pretty short, so don't think you can spend the first round getting to know the system, you must touch the ground running here. All this means that there's a severe experience penalty, beginners will be up against an insurmountable disadvantage when playing against those who have played before. I do hope some effort went into balancing the special player powers, they're completely wild.

RATING: 7
You need to explore the world, but you only have five turns to do it. It's a dice-worker game, where different numbers let you do different things. You can choose to focus on contracts or exploration; exploration is very expensive but likely to allow you different actions, while contracts are probably a little easier, but it's difficult to do a lot of them.  It's a little harder to accomplish anything in this game than I'd like.  The different roles with their powers are really cool; they are all powerful and there are lots of them. (2 plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I really love the available decisions and resource management. The cost of movement feels different than a lot of games, and trading goods around to fulfill contracts is both satisfying, and seems to be thematically appropriate. Variable player powers are drastically different, and all seem like they have good paths to victory. My biggest criticism would be against the component size for gold bars, silk, pepper and camels. The size difference between the small (value=1) and big (value=3) wooden markers is not easy to detect without very close inspection.

RATING: 6.5
Suggest: Enthusiastic

Initial rating. 

It's the kind of Euro dice game that put all info on the board, with the only undetermined thing is dice rolls. As there are so many seemingly viable choices on a single turn, no doubt there is over-analyzed issue.

The travel and contract actions give most points of game, both of which are very costly, thus unlike most Euro, it feels very hard to push the VP track forward.

I don't find the dice-placement being especially intriguing, it's not bad but too much arithmetic. I will play more to find out if I like it.

RATING: 3.5
GCL: https://boardgamegeek.com/listitem/4041940?commentid=5649960#comment5649960

So, Marco Polo...

I've had almost 24 hours to try to shed my frustration at this game. I knew going in it was a bit of a risk, but it seemed like it could work. And even after playing it, we both look at each other and say, "It's so close!" I think we are not the right audience for it.

The rules are lengthy, and seem pretty well written. However, I really wish they had had us playtest this game, and we would have pointed out the confusing parts and inconsistencies. For example, some things can be done the number of times as pips on the dice, and in some cases, that doesn't matter. In some cases the pips tell you which things to consider, and you do it once. Some things you can only do once. And yet they use the same bracket arrow symbol, and no differentiation on the card. 

Do not use the same symbol to mean very different things. 

Or at least, differentiate it with a secondary symbol so it's clear that it can be done the number of pips on the die/dice, or a x1 to show only once. Or something.

But, let me go back a bit.

Everybody had 5 dice, you roll them, and then you put them on spots on the board to do that thing. Some spots require 1 die, some 2 or 3. Most you can use if an opponent is there, you just pay extra (the value of the lowest die you are using). Some spaces (favor track), as far as we can tell, you can't share, there are just 4 spaces. Again, the same bordered dice space that works a certain way should not be used when it doesn't work that way. It should have the standard 1-die frame, meaning you need to use one die, and have a "limit of 4" notation, if only 4 dice can be used there. Or something.

I was frustrated enough at all of the symbol weirdness that I wanted to shake my fist at it and say, "Your theme is pasted on!" Really, it wasn't my main issue with the game, but it seemed a decent insult to hurl at it. 

Oh, have to go back again. I said that everybody had 5 dice, and you roll them. Hah, not true. The first player gets a character card where you don't roll your dice, you get to set them to be whatever you want (?!?). Why would I play a dice-rolling game where I don't roll dice. And why would the first player (in the beginning game) get that ability? Maybe the 3rd or 4th player. There are character cards that give advantages, like extra dice. 

Maybe, this qualifies as asymmetrical play for Bryan, and the dice and blocking and whatnot are Troyes-enough for him. So maybe, this would all turn him on. We did not play with the character cards, because they annoyed us, but we got to the end and very little had happened, so we thought, maybe we would have been able to do more if we had used the character cards. But we didn't want to. So we were in a bind. 

So you have dice with pips, either rolled or chosen, sometimes 5 but maybe more, and you use them to get goods (gold, silk, spices, camels). Mostly you get them using a big chart. There is a row of gold, row of silk, row of spices, row of camels. Then there are columns for 1 pip, 2, pip, 3 pip, 4 pip, etc. The pip is the lowest face of the die or dice you use. Gold needs 3 dice, silk 2, and spices and camels 1. So one die with 2 pips might get you 2 spices or 2 camels, but you'd need 2 dice, the lower one with 2 pips, to get 2 silk, and 3 dice, lowest 2 pips, to get 2 gold. If Sam already used that row, and I wanted silk, I may want to play 4, 4, 3, so that I get what's on the 3 spot (could be 2 silk and a coin, something like that) but, since that spot is occupied, I have to pay the value of my lowest die. So I'd have to pay 3 coins to use that spot. 

So a few of the areas are about getting things, like the goods and coins. Then there is a place to get contracts. 3 camels, 2 gold, 2 spices get 5 gold and 4 VP, something like that. Also, most completed contracts gets 7 VP. I had given up on travelling (explained next), so just churned through contracts, giving me the bonus.

The other thing you can do is travel, move around on the map on the top half of the board. To travel, unless you get a bonus somewhere, you have to go to the travel spot. You place 2 dice, and again, it's the lowest value that tells you what value you can use, but it also costs money, so even if you have the ability to move 3 spots, you might only have money for 1 or 2 spots (as often happened to us). Or even if by some miracle you had the dice and money, there may be extra costs on the map (camels, money) and so that might limit your travel. I pretty much stayed in a hotel in one city most of the game. At the beginning you get 2 bonus cards with 2 cities (think Ticket to Ride), with bonus points if you put a house on each city (okay, not a house, but wooden structure meeple). There's also a bonus table for building houses in cities on your cards, 1/2/3/4, so even though I only built 1, I got a few points for it. 

Building a house in a small city gives income of some sort every turn (5 coins, 3 coins and a camel, etc.). First person to build in a large city gets the bonus (2 silk, etc.) If you build all of your houses but 1, there's a bonus (I think 5VP), all of the houses, 10VP. We were in no danger of that. There's also a city where the first person to reach it gets 10VP, the second (7?), third (4?), fourth (1?). Sam made it there, so he got 10 VP. 

The artwork is lovely (the score track is beautiful), the components are good quality, both cardboard and wood. The character cards were tiles rather than cards. We wanted to like this game. It comes really close to a game we would like. But it's not there for us.

RATING: 2.5
Given my gaming taste, reading about this game it should be one of those games I really like, Played twice, first time face-to-face, next on-line, but in both games I was stymied by continual unlucky die-rolls and there is nothing I could  do when that happens continually.

A mish-mash of mechanisms and poorly designed to balance piss-poor luck with the dice makes this a game (?) that I will certainly never play again; and that level of disquiet with a game is normally reserved for war games and those "dripping with theme" but forgot about the fun of the game play. 

With its absurdly high rating, this is another game which proves that some people can be fooled all of the time, even those whose gaming tastes are similar to mine.

RATING: 6
This was a nice game but I never felt like I was in control of my destiny. I don't necessarily feel like the randomness was too much, I think that was sufficiently mitigatable, but I did feel like between the location goals and the guy you get you're pretty heavily directed in certain ways. Because of this most of the game felt to me like optimizing the single turn, I very rarely was thinking even one turn ahead. It felt very much like I was on rails. Not an unpleasant experience and certainly not something I'd avoid but I'm not planning on going looking for it either. But there is some fun in using your player power and the special actions on the travel areas.

RATING: 8
2015-08-17

Initial Rating: 7.0 (August 2015)

Good Euro game. I wasn’t expecting anything from it but I really enjoyed it. The rules aren’t complex and the playtime can be under 90 minutes with fast players. Still it has lots of interesting/deep decisions (basically where to play your dice, but there are many options to do this). The game is very tight as you only have 5 rounds so it has a feel of “too much to do with too few resources”, money is very scarce (and traveling is very expensive). 

It has some luck, but I think it can be mitigated (rerolls, low dice are very useful, if the sum of the dice is 15 or less you can get money/camels) so it doesn’t bother me. There is a higher luck factor with the goal cards but I don’t think they are a deal breaker. The replayability seems to be very high, a huge plus in my book. 

The art is beautiful and the game components are very well done.

I don’t like nor dislike the theme.

On the bad side, some characters seem much more powerful than others. In particular the one that let you travel through oasis practically guarantees that you will get first to Beijing AND you will be able to accomplish all your goal cards. The other one I felt was overly powerful is the one that lets you play your dice without paying any money. To conclude, I feel the characters are unbalanced but I’m not really sure. I need to play more to really see. 
 
Bottom line, The Voyages of Marco Polo is one of the best dice placement games I have played, it offers lots of strategy and I love that. I want to play it soon again. A keeper in my game collection.

Current Rating: 7.0
[hr]
2016-01-01

After more plays, I still can find a noticeable unbalance in the characters and I’m very glad :D. They seem unbalanced but they aren’t. 

The variable (advanced) setup add lots of replayability. Very good stuff.

For now I’m enjoying the game tremendously so I’m upgrading its rating. I will never turn down a game of Marco Polo, very good game.

RATING: 9
- Really enjoy this game.
- Looks great on the table. Haven't played with 2, but plays really well with 3-4.
- I really enjoy the puzzle of making the most out of the roll while also trying to build an engine, achieve goals, fulfill contracts, and block/avoid being blocked by opponents.
- The very different, and very powerful player powers make this a cool game as everyone is jealous of everyone else's abilities.
- The dice-roll randomness can be frustrating, especially as there isn't too much you can do about it that isn't prohibitively expensive.
- There's also a bit of randomness in the dealing of the goals that can be a little bit frustrating in that some combinations are harder/easier to achieve than others. This is somewhat balanced by the points awarded, but in combination with the player powers, it can definitely appear that one player has an advantage/disadvantage over others.
- However, it looks like the soon to be released expansion will help rectify/balance some of these issues.

RATING: 7
A solid dice-placement euro whose unique feature is giving everyone a different rule-breaking "super power" that pretty much allows them to ignore one or more of the games core rules when playing. The actions involve collecting resources in order to fulfill contracts or to travel to destinations on the board, which open up additional actions for the players that reach them. While points are acquired throughout the game through contracts, end game scoring involves reaching destination cities on two goal cards handed out at the game's beginning.

Besides the rule-breaking characters, there isn't a ton of innovative stuff in the game. It is, however, solidly designed and pleasant to play. I'm not sure if the interest in this will last more than a few plays, but we'll see. I'll enjoy it for now and my fiance liked it as well, which is a plus.

RATING: 8
Although I had my expectations high, The Voyages of Marco Polo did not disappoint. 

The game has been sitting on my shelf for a while, but I decided that I would not teach myself the rules.  Invariably I make monumental rules mistakes that I don't catch for several games.  There is a lot going on in Marco Polo, so I would have certainly made some rules blunders.  I finally came across someone who could teach me the game and we played two players.

In Marco Polo, there are many good choices, but if your opponent selects something you had hoped to do, suddenly it becomes a little more expensive. With only six turns in a game, you are forced to accomplish a lot in a short period of time, so you must be judicious with your choices. 

As you secure trading posts, you give yourself more options for die placement. If you can secure the right cities, you maybe able to create an engine that not only rewards you with victory points and goods.

There are a few potential pitfalls.  At the begining of the game is character selection.  Each character has special abilities and not all the characters are equally good. Frankly, a few of the characters are just plan bad.  Maybe the bad characters could be used as a balancing mechanism, when playing with new players.

The only other potential concern is the player count. The game is very snappy at two players. I would not have any worries with three players, but at four players, I am afraid the game may drag on a bit.  Also, one of the characters seems to be overly strong at the higher player counts - it mitigates the additional cost of entering a blocked space.

Looking forward to future plays.  I anticipate my rating to increase over time.players take the better characters and experienced players select the relatively bad characters.

Also, one of the characters seems to be overly strong at the higher player counts - it mitigates the additional cost of entering a blocked space.

Looking forward to future plays.
