RATING: 7
Bit of love/hate with this one.  It uses dice in a similar manner as Bora Bora - you play your own dice as workers.  High dice are more powerful.  However, in Bora Bora, the advantage of low dice is that they are easier to play.  Here, every player after the first in a given area simply pays the value of the dice.  It's a bit of all-or-nothing.  Not as clever tactically as Bora Bora.  

I sometimes feel that the game is a bit of a grind.  You want to do more than you can, but rather than a delicious frustration and challenge, I find that the gears just grind down with turns that are not sufficiently productive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played Marco Polo several times and while I think the game is excellent it just doesn't grab me. I have no explanation. I'm happy to play if someone suggests it, but I would seldom suggest it myself.

[b]Constraints:[/b]
The game does an excellent job of squeezing you in every direction. There are never enough actions to accomplish everything and money is extremely tight. The area in which I really feel squeezed is movement. Planning and executing movement is very though, and if you want to make your routes you are going to have to plan things carefully from the first turn. Superb and challenging!

[b]Dice:[/b]
While the dice occasionally hinder you, it's usually just an interesting puzzle to work out. I would lump this with [thing=84876]The Castles of Burgundy[/thing]. Both games feature dice prominently and both more-or-less remove the ability to be completely screwed by them.

[b]Asymmetry:[/b]
The character you get in the game will have a huge impact on your play and strategy. This provides a lot of variability between games and tweaks how you play each time. They seem very well balanced and meaningfully different, which is quite an accomplishment.

RATING: 6

RATING: 9.5

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I really enjoy the blend of mechanics in this game, the dice placement, route building and the unique player powers make for crucial decisions every turn. The 80 minute play time for 4 players is accurate and the game has a great pace and doesn't overstay its welcome. The dice rarely screw you over because high and low dice values are equally desirable, the dice rolls lend a tactical element to each turn because you have to find the most effective actions for the dice you've rolled.

The variability of the big city action spaces and the route cards keep the game fresh, let alone how the player abilities completely change how each game feels. The traveling around the map is tough but it's satisfying when you make clever combinations with the city action spaces and complete a bunch of contracts or travel multiple times in a turn.

Each dice action, each camel and every bit of money matters, the game is very tight. I've taught the game many times now and the first play usually people will stick to contracts and won't complete very much because they often spend many dice on getting gold or silk. Once people get experienced there are a few things that make be game easier to complete many contracts and traveling a lot at the same time.

One thing is the rewards that each contract gives, more often than not picking the right sequence of contracts can let you 'chain' them together, where one contract reward lets you complete the next one easier. Or if traveling is your primary goal then choose the contracts that give you camels, extra travel or money as rewards.

The biggest element to pay attention to in my opinion is before you actually start playing the game. The first things that are set up are the big city action cards, bonus tiles and the small city round bonus tiles. These are hugely important and should determine which route cards you choose. For example if you see that there are many big city action cards that provide camels then you'll know traveling the top two paths will be easier, if you see that there are not many money spaces available then you know to avoid the bottom route (the one with the expensive ferry routes).

I had assumed I would like it based on the previews I read but it exceeded my expectations. The designer duo had previously brought out Tzolk'in which is another one of my favourites. Luciani was also behind Grand Austria Hotel another acclaimed Euro from 2015. Really looking forward to what this duo comes out with next!

The Voyages of Marco Polo has quickly become one of my favourite games and definitely my favourite game of 2015. If you like tight euro-style games that require an equal amount of strategic planning and tactical play then I can't recommend this game enough.

RATING: 8
A fairly straight forward game in that there are only so many actions you can do, certainly at the start of the game however the challenge comes from working out the most efficient actions that work towards your goal. Whilst other players can't block the main actions, following them incurs a tax based upon the die you use.

The various characters and player abilities should give this game a lot of replay ability and makes me want to play it again with other characters, In this game there doesn't appear to be such a thing as a bad roll as both high and low numbers have their advantages.

RATING: 7
Sold after nine plays. Still a good game but it isn't one I find myself asking to play or get to the table. While there are different routes to pursue depending on the character and the variable map setup, it still feels samey. Perhaps I'm just getting a bit jaded, who knows?

Repeat plays have been quite interesting and enjoyable. Works well with two players. Feels like it basically comes down to a fight between whomever uses their overpowered character most efficiently in respect to reading and using the board's setup as to who will win.

FIRST IMPRESSION:

I enjoyed my first play quite a bit and we jumped right into the advanced game. Didn't realize initially how difficult movement can be on the board. In the character draft, I am thinking that you want to make sure to focus your strategy around your character as much as possible since they all "break the rules" in some fashion. Some feel more powerful than others, but each are powerful in their own way if used well. The dice manipulation and use is quite interesting and quite frustrating at times as you cannot do everything you want to do most times. The small town and large city bonuses and special actions are quite interesting and the city actions will vary quite a bit from game to game as you only use 9 of 30 cards in a game. There's quite a bit to suss out in this game as you figure out the best plan of attack for you. Most spots can be occupied by everyone but after the first person places dice, players are paying gold equal to the lowest die placed on top of another player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
+ Very nice components, perfectly designed for usability.
+ Relatively inexpensive. 
+ Streamlined rules. Almost everything is on the board (except for the rules on stacking dice, which are important but simple enough).
+ Nice player aids. 
+ All the powers are attractive. 
+ Each game should be different thanks to the initial random placement of bonuses (to be checked with more than one game).
+ Individual player powers add to the variety. 
+ Nice tension created by the goals. 
+ Works well enough with two, even though I guess that more players bring more constraints, which is probably nice (not tried yet). 
+ The goals of each player are very clear (face up contracts), which makes strategizing around this possible (through blocking actions, etc.).
+ The race element keeps the tension high enough (real-time scoring, with a race for the city bonuses).
+ Fast turns.
- This is only another Euro, albeit an extremely well crafted one. 
$ Not as deep as Agricola (because Agricola has a rich set of cards)—but with a larger "mechanical" variety thanks to the random distribution of powers on the map: sometimes it will be easier to travel thanks to easily accessible recurring camel bonuses, sometimes not, etc.).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Like talking to a scientist at a party where everyone just wants have fun. If only she'd let her hair down and open up a bit. There's a fun game in here but it refuses to let itself out. 

The game is way too tight and over just as it starts to get going. Moving as a strategy is too expensive for most characters and the value of moving is not huge even if you do it. The benefits moving unlocks are minimal, by the time you unlock them the game is almost over and often the most useful benefits don't even appear due to the random setup.

The superb production quality and the mechanics make the game seem interesting but you soon realise that the game winner will generally be the one who rolls the highest numbers.

Remove the costs of movement on the board, add large bonuses to every city regardless of size, make the bonuses more useful, make moving through a city enough to place a market or add more movement paths and possibly add a couple of extra rounds and you might have a game worth playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
What's Great: 
Excellent feeling of balance with all of the bonus actions to compensate for bad dice rolls. Numerous strategies, various characters and cards which add to its replayability. Overall just a great gaming experience and simplistic enough to teach to both casual and competitive players. 

Even if you feel like you're losing, you can come back from behind by fulfilling goals and placing all of your trading posts.

What's Not So Great: 
Out of print (at the time of review) so an affordable copy may be hard to find. Certain actions may feel redundant in certain play throughs especially if people are playing very nicely.

Verdict:
Easily one of my favorite games of all time and will stay in my collection!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I like that although there's a lot of randomness in [b][thing=171623][/thing][/b], most of it is in the set up and once set up, the only other bit is the die and how the contracts come out. And the randomness of both is mitigated by, for the dice, using camels to re-roll or modify them, and for the contracts, by there being six available each turn. It's very rare that all the available contracts are unappealing.  The random board layout makes each game feel just a bit different. Some games money is plentiful and other times it's very tight. Some games, travel is easy as there are locations and contracts that give movement points aplenty, and some games there aren't. In other games, VPs can be made in cities, and other games, VPs are primarily from contracts. Which means each play there's a different optimum strategy (which may or may not have to be flexible given what other players are doing).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very good traditional euro. More control than Kingsburg, and more thematic than Castles of Burgandy, makes this my preferred euro dice game.
The only downside being that it's too long and somewhat prone to AP, esp in the endgame.
Edited on 17/8/2015: On further thinking I believe this game offers nothing new, but is a perfect mix of Castles of Burgundy, Elfenland, some strange worker placement and maybe a bit of Terra Mystica. The theme is very thin, but the colourful board, super functional design and multiple paths of victory all make this game enjoyable.

RATING: 8
Regrettably, I’ve only played a single game of this so far, but that certainly whetted my appetite. While your character will certainly pull you towards a certain strategy, there are more than enough decisions left in how best to implement this. The fact that each of these characters seems to appear overpowered to somebody speaks towards an overall good balance, and replayability should be through the roof due to the variant setup.

My only (small) worry is a potential lack of interaction – in our one game, one player made a costly move  to thwart the strategy of another, which did not work out well for him. Generally, though, the boons you gain as the first merchant to reach a city should make such moves palatable, and there is a good deal of possible blocking moves in the action spaces. So on the whole, I’d say this is another winner from Hans im Glück, and might be one of the few games I’m going to buy in 2015.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I had very high expectations for this game! It was a MUST HAVE, based on my affinity for dice placement games and the general look of the game. I was not disappointed. Don't be fooled--there is a lot going on in this game. If you don't pay attention to everything, you might get creamed! Being that the game is titled: "The Voygaes of Marco Polo," you should fully expect that traveling is essential in this game. In fact, you shouldn't see your dice placement options as varying paths to victory; you should see these options as opportunities to collect the necessary resources and materials to make your journey successful. It reminded me of Village a bit. Their central mechanisms are quite different, but in both games traveling is extremely powerful. In Village though, traveling is one of several options. In this game, traveling is the end goal, and everything else is the means to get you there. 

The components are quite nice in this game. I love the resource bits and the little camels. Even the meeples and trading posts are nicely produced--so much better than simple cubes. The art is beautiful. The graphic design is nice and clear, however, because the board is so busy, it's difficult to see everything that's going on during the game. 

The theme is expressed by the components and art. The special ability tiles also help. However, on a thematic immersion scale, I'd give this game a 4 out of 10. To be fair, it might feel more thematic as you get more comfortable with the game mechanics and options. 

I'm very happy to own this game. I love it! My initial rating is only tentative, and I fully expect it to go up with future plays.

RATING: 8.5
Bought at cardhaus.

Simple gameplay and dice placement mechanisms, but a lot of strategy.  Each character feels vastly overpowered (esp. Rashid), but it's still somehow balanced.  This creates a sense of asymmetric gameplay.  Combined with the variable setup and differing city powers, there's A LOT of replayability.

RATING: 6
Game ổn từ đầu tới cuối, không có gì quá đặc biệt cũng không có gì dở.

Điểm cộng của game là khả năng chơi lại cực kỳ cao, mỗi lần chơi game này sẽ là một trải nghiệm hoàn toàn khác. Vì với mỗi game việc set up các action có thể làm được trong game là khác nhau hoàn toàn và mỗi nhân vật trong game có một skill riêng, mà phải nói là skill rất mạnh và định hình chiến thuật cho nhân vật đó. Nên nói mỗi khi chơi game này là chơi một game khác hẳn so với ván trước cũng ko sai. Vậy nên chiến thuật trong game này không sâu mà là dàn trải ra rất nhiều thể loại.

Game có độ khó trung bình, không burn não. Việc quản lý dice để làm action khá thú vị. Khả năng bóp của game là cao, vì những action mạnh cũng như thiên về kiếm điểm đều nằm trong large city, mỗi lượt chỉ có 1 người được dùng. Cũng như các action kia khi người ta đi trước rồi thì mình khá thọt khi phải trả thêm tiền để đi tương ứng với xí ngầu mình dùng. Nên đến vòng cuối khi cần vét những điểm cuối cùng và nhiều khi là cần vét cú chót một số lượng điểm lớn thì rất dễ bị bóp chẹt bởi những người chơi khác.

Điểm trừ của game là thematic yếu, nói trắng ra chỉ là một đám lái buôn với super power đi phát triển mạng lưới trading port và buôn bán kiếm tiền trên con đường tơ lụa. Game scale không được tốt lắm, chơi với 2p thì game cũng chỉ hay ở mức 8đ, còn với 3-4 ng thì game có thể lên đến 9đ nếu may mắn trong việc setup ngẫu nhiên.

RATING: 10
A fantastic dice placement game where every player has fantastic near game breaking ability, anything from taking goods when other players purchase something from the market to not paying penalties when placing on occupied spaces to choosing your dice result when placing one, and so forth.

In the game you try to gather goods and money in order to travel (to set up trading posts and hopefully visit as many of the cities depicted on your scoring card) and fulfill contracts (to earn points). After a given amount of rounds the player with the most points wins the game.

It's highly enjoyable, especially because of the really good player powers that are in the game. It's often really hard to decide as you kinda just want all of them.

Highly recommended, it's a complete blast to play.

RATING: 7.5
Dice-driven worker placement that inn many ways is a puzzle because you're trying to maximize what you can do given your dice, the routes open on the board, actions open, character powers, contracts, etc. Of course your opponents will often mess with your plans. You can travel around the map opening up new actions and gaining goods but it will cost you camels (to cross the dessert) or coins (to cross water) not to mention the cost just to hire a caravan. Instead, you may try to concentrate on just collecting goods to fulfill contracts, or you can try to do a bit of both. One can also purchase dice, change die values, etc. There's really a lot to think about and the designers have combined some concepts we're all familiar with in interesting ways.

RATING: 8.9
And this one is a great great game... the thing that sets it apart from other dice placement games like Kingsburg, Alien Frontiers, etc is the insanely powerful game-breaking characters... they are so crazy powerful that all of them seem a bit broken and that makes the game balanced... makes the different strategies be approached in different ways...
In what game that you roll dice to be able to do actions and the value of the dice have a direct impact on your choices do you have a character that allows you to go the whole game without rolling a single die?!?! You can just say what the value of the die is when you place it! That's just MADNESS!! But it's also crazy fun!

The rest of the game is pretty straight forward with one difference for the normal dice/worker placement games because usually when a place is occupied no one else can use that spot... in this game there's only one place where you can get limited by the value of the dice already placed there, you have to place a die equal or higher than the previous die, in all other places you can place any die you want (or combination of dice) and do the action... the thing is that if you are not the first to do the action you'll have to pay to the bank the same amount of money has the value of the lowest die you are using to do that action.

Great great game wit lots of freedom and yet a lot of good decisions to be made and the crazy insane fun powers are just awesome! 

The game has an immense replay-ability because of the different Characters, there are 8 different ones in the game... there are a LOT of personal goal cards... there are a lot of cit cards that will be placed in the cities with a new place for players that reached that city to do the action... etc

No two games will be the same!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I've been enjoying this one a lot more than I expected. It is initially frustrating because the cost of movement is SO HIGH--it is very easy to get locked-in if you can't figure out how to snag camels and money. Also, the radically different starting characters seem pretty unbalanced: aren't Marco Polo and Berke Khan heavily favoured (free contract + free die / don't pay for already-taken actions))? Yet if you didn't have some kind of character power this game would be a real slog: I would not advise playing it without the characters.

But I like puzzles and this game is best approached as a puzzle: how do I get from A to B to C with the resources I have, and taking into account the possibility of other players reaching cities or grabbing placement spots before I can? The game has an interestingly disparate mechanism for worker placement--you can take most spots even if they are occupied, as long as you pay some money, but the city spots (which are the best ones) can only be used by one player, period.

Not sure how this one will hold up over the long run but for now let's give it an 8 [dropped to 7.5]. It plays quite briskly, unlike the monstrously slow Grand Austria Hotel by the same designers, though it will slow down a lot with AP-prone players given how much hangs on each decision.

UPDATE: I still rather like this one, even though it's very low interaction--aside from snagging contracts someone else wants or making them pay more for die placements, there is actually no interaction. That's OK in my view--sometimes games just use other players as a kind of constraint/extra obstacle. 

My main tip: don't get too greedy about the black dice--unless you genuinely need extra dice (for the gold spot, e.g.), you'll just end up buying extra camels to pay for them which isn't much of a deal. They can be handy for guaranteeing you move last, though--which makes you the starting player next round. -- Also, snag a few trading posts ASAP because the actions they offer are at so much better die-to-value ratios than the main board.

I am guessing with experience I will learn to max out the limited time and dice.  Either way this game lived up to the hype I heard and will hit the table often.

RATING: 7.5
In this worker placement game, players roll five dice which act as their workers. Based on the theme of early 13th century Marco Polo era, players gather resources, fulfill contracts or travel. The neat trick is that you can stack dice on other people's dice, but your cost to do that action is the pips on the die. You also get a special character in the game which gives you a special ability. Over five rounds, players earn points for contracts, or travelling.

I enjoyed this game from the same designers of Tzolkin. It dawned on me that you try to base your strategy around the special ability of your character. After a few games in, I'm starting to see a bit of the complexities with travelling, and the bonus tokens on some cities. It's fun and I'm looking forward to more plays.

RATING: 8
Worker placement game where dice are the workers, and there are restrictions of placement dependent on the dice throw. Different goods, and a transportation unit (camels) can become available and also can be traded for VPs. The game is asymmetric, each player has a character with differing abilities chosen at the beginning of the game. This element really makes the game. There is also an exploration element on a map, providing more goods and VPs.
The exploration seems weaker than the trading, but it is mostly not, therefore it should not be ignored.

Strengths: Have to plan your strategy, the order of travel, the source of money, goods and victory points. Variation of cards - that determine trading possibilities on the map -, of playing characters, of placement of small town bonuses ensure replayability for a long time.

Weaknesses: In some setups a certain character may be overpowered, therefore first choice can decide the whole game. Lucky or unlucky dice throws can decide a game.

RATING: 9
I must admit to being a little biased because my wife got it for me for my birthday. But despite being a dice worker placement, which is not usually at the top of my list, this game really works for me.

It's streamlined and clean, the luck of the dice rolls is rarely a factor, and there's a good amount of engine building, goods collection and a little spatial element. The game rewards strategical analysis of the board and careful selection of the asymmetric characters (another plus). All the elements are tied together really nicely, and the game doesn't outstay its welcome.

About the only thing negative I would say is that there's nothing really innovative; the "soft" blocking is from Leonardo da Vinci, the dice from Alien Frontiers and the card powers from Troyes. But I don't care for innovation for innovation's sake (ironically, I like Innovation), so I'm not too bothered.

Update: bumped up rating by 1/2 a point because I've now played it 6-7 times, and every time I want to play it more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
The components are nice, wooden pieces of good quality and they have their own shapes, the dice feel a bit light, and the board art is quite good (it includes 2 minor errata that don't affect the gameplay at all).

It's a medium to heavy route building and dice placement eurogame. The rules are not very elegant, they have minor twists and exceptions, but they are not difficult to understand and memorize. The mechanisms are nice and they are combined in an interesting way, but they are not original. The theme... Well, there is some traveling, and that's all :p

For me, its best feature is the asymmetry of the players: each one has a different power. But it's not that they have a colorful minor skill or advantage, they have a VERY POWERFUL ability that has a REALLY DEEP impact in the gameplay. When the skills are that different, you can't avoid talking about balance: people always tend to think the powers of the other players are better. I try to be objective here, and I think Matteo Polo is the worse of the suggested initial characters. Also, I think than Mercator and BerkeKhan are slightly better than Raschid. But this is just my first impression, surely the designers have tested this thoroughly and I'm wrong.

It's not an easy game to figure out. It's difficult to see the correlation in points of some of your actions, especially the traveling part.

My main concerns:
-> The powers granted by the Cities (mainly the Small Cities bonus tiles) are very different in terms of power and utility. The first players have an advantage because it's likely they will be able to travel first and take those tiles that grant powers for the rest of the game.
-> Players choices will affect your action and you will have to adapt your strategy and decisions during your turn. It has not been the fast game some players promised it would be.

The luck of the dice is there, but it's not that relevant. Low rolls are also useful, sometimes necessary.

There are enough variable elements to ensure replayability, but probably the game will lose some of its appeal when you experience (directly or indirectly) all the character powers. We'll see how the game holds up after further plays, but for the moment, it's a candidate for my best game of the year.

But of course, having played only once, I don't know the first of it :whistle:

Impressions atfer first play [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3925993#item3925993]here[/url].

RATING: 9
Voyages of Marco Polo is dice worker-placement game that revolves around traveling through the silk road and fulfilling business contracts and it is just marvelous!

and now, the bad, the good, and the great (in that order)
bad: the rules are complicated to teach, partially because of insufficient explanations in the rulebook and also because of lot of exceptions to given rules (but on the other hand, the board more than helps you in understanding your available actions).
good: it is built on familiar principles from other games and while it does not bring any new ones, use of its mechanisms is clever and they make a lot of sense game-wise. Also components are done very well and the coloring really helps with immersion.
great: it is tons of fun! the theme is done perfectly, big plus (at least for me) is that the characters are real historical figures, variability of the setup is done very well and helps replayability along with different powers for your characters. And the special abilities of your characters are really interesting, powerful and absolutely gamechanging! You can be a missonary that can "jump" through oasis, merchant who receives goods for every market transaction made by other players etc. And if you dont like luck in dice rolling games, you can play as a character that chooses his own results! How crazy is that!:) really the best thing is, that all of these powers sound really game-braking but somehow they are balanced pretty well, which gives a lot of credit to ingenuity of the designers. The gameplay has quite the tempo and it also plays really well with 2. One thing that makes it a great game though, is the depth it offers. The more you play it, the more options and strategies you discover, so the game stays interesting even after repeated plays.
all in all, awesome game.

edit: have to increase the rating, as this is slowly becoming one of my all time favourites.

RATING: 8.75
https://drive.google.com/file/d/0By3RmyRN2h-tdC16cTJNWmx6LW8/view?usp=sharing
規則:
*洗勻18張目標卡，6小城市版塊按字母放置，大城市版塊隨機放7個，剩下的不用，大城市卡隨機放9張，剩下的不用，起始契約卡放旁邊，然後抽30張形成五疊，一疊六張，剩下的蓋著放旁邊成為特殊契約區，第一疊開在契約區，五顆黑骰放在市集區上方，跑最多國家的起始
*每個玩家拿對應的顏色五顆骰子，房子放在個人圖版上，拿一個起始契約，起始兩個駱駝，金錢照順位增加1，首位7元，首次玩建議角色，兩個人一個放分數，一個放起始地點，每個玩家抽兩張目標卡
*遊戲進行五輪，每輪分成6個階段，之後最後記分
1.決定起始玩家 由最後進行旅行的玩家成為此回合的起始玩家，首輪隨便，沒人的話順位不變
2.小城市跟角色收入
3.玩家把骰子拿回手上
4.玩家擲骰並放在圖版上(總和小於15，差值可拿對應的金錢或駱駝，可隨意搭配)
5.輪流進行回合，每回合必須做一次強制動作，前後可進行額外動作
***強制動作 放骰子進行動作，每一個動作每輪自己顏色的骰子只能出現一次，一放就執行該動作，骰面最小的骰子決定可執行幾次或是拿對應資源，行動可少作，但至少一次
A不管骰面都拿五元
B市集 放對應顆數骰子，按骰面最小的拿對應欄資源，可拿同行較少的那欄
C漢賞賜 放的骰子要大於等於前面的，拿資源三選一跟兩駱駝
D獲得契約 選小於骰面的1~2個契約，如果自己的擺滿可棄到特殊契約牌庫底
*契約拿完往左推齊，如果全部抽完從特殊契約牌庫抽兩張補
E旅行 放兩顆骰跟舉骰面小的 決定最多可移動的步數，付錢，根據移動過的地點付駱駝或錢，停在城市就自動蓋交易站(一個城市最多一個人一個)
*有交易站的大城市，該行動你可用第一個蓋的還可拿額外好處，小城市直接獲得上面收入，且每回合可在拿，移動方向沒有限制，如果全放完又需要放交易站則改為移動交易站
*大城市行動一輪只能一個人使用，轉換率看骰點
***額外動作***
A完成契約 獲得獎勵並蓋著在遊戲最後比數目
B一顆骰換三元
C一駱駝重骰一顆骰
D兩駱駝 骰點+-1，6跟1不能互換
E三駱駝獲得一顆黑骰並骰跟之後可放，一回合只能做一次
*如果要使用別人使用過的行動，支付骰點數較小的那顆骰對應金錢，就可把骰放在別人骰上使用該行動
6.輪結束，清空契約，換新的
*最後記分，平手比駱駝數
1.目標卡記分，每張目標卡如果上面兩個地點都完成就有分，下面則是指兩張目標卡上不同地點到過幾個，或得分數
2.10元一分
3.到北京的拿北京分，且可兩資源轉一分，駱駝不能轉
4.完成最多契約的加七分
**2-3人
3人 黑骰四顆 漢賞賜格放一顆點數一的中立骰
2人 黑骰三顆 漢賞賜格放兩顆點數一的中立骰，一顆中立骰放在拿五元的位置
**進階規則***
*小城鎮版塊隨機放
*洗勻角色卡，開玩家數加一張，尾家開始選角色
*目標卡抽四選二
**角色卡
1.可指定骰點
2.每輪開始多一白骰跟一個契約(從特殊契約抽)
3.當有人拿市場 或漢賞賜 或五元 可獲得好處
4.北京開始
5.兩個人 每輪開始一駱駝
6.放有人放的動作不用付錢
7.起始多兩棟交易所，經過城鎮就可以蓋，11棟蓋完加10分
8.每輪收入三元，可花一個移動任意在綠洲間移動

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Beautiful game with mechanics and flourishes borrowed from others: specific character/player powers (Terra Mystica), a map with a limited amount of spaces for players to earn points(Hansa Teutonica), "contracts"/quests with resources for points (Lords of Waterdeep), and dice placement for actions(Bora Bora). Like most of Z-man's collection, Voyages of Marco Polo is malleable to many different strategies but the ones that utilize their own character power tend to be the most profitable because the game can be very punishing. It can feel very Feld-ian ie., there is so much to do in the game and a player has to completely ignore some sections of the board to focus and garner the most points; I just didn't have enough time or actions to complete everything on the board. I'd like to play this game again. It was a fun and enjoyable (mercifully short Euro) experience! 

After playing a couple more times with the expansion characters and getting a feel for the general strategy, I have fallen in love with this game even more now. What I really appreciate about the game is that the game end scoring never changes; there's no mysterious "blue shell" mechanic that messes up the best laid plans to win. Marco Polo has gone up in my estimation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
"Excellent low-confrontation euro that plays pretty quickly, with plenty of room for clever combos. What sets it apart are the super-powerful player roles."

What I like:
- Player role superpowers
- Low confrontation, with less hard blocking than typical worker placement
- Clever combos
- Thematic enough
- Forces a lot of really interesting and tough decisions, but avoids heavy AP
- Strong both in tactical play and long-term strategies
- Plays quickly for its depth

What I don't like:
- Bigger resource pieces for 3x aren't different enough
- Dice places on board should indicate placement rules more clearly
- Transformations should visually indicate whether they are once or once-per-pip
- A couple player scaling rules (or non-rules) seem a bit funky, but I haven't tried 2p so might end up disagreeing with myself on this one

After my 3rd game, I bumped my rating up from an 8 to a 9. 

The 20-30 minutes per player estimate seems accurate, even for learning games. I think the BGG-voted weight of 3.2 might be just a bit high.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The Voyages of Marco Polo is a difficult game--and that's a good thing. Points are hard to come by, movement costs seem insurmountable at the start of the game, and the five game turns feel incredibly limiting. Despite this, there are a huge variety of different approaches you can take to this game, and those approaches will change every game because of the variable player powers and board spaces. What's common in all of it, though, is the need to form a plan--you won't be able to go to very many places (usually 5 turns will mean 5 places if you plan it out right), so you'll need to carefully decide which action spaces you want to open up for yourself, which income spaces are essential, and which cities to go after because they're on your route cards. Unlike other dice placement games, other people don't block you out of spots, but they might make it more expensive. Really, there's a ton to explore in this game, and I feel like I've only scratched the surface.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 10
Quickly becoming a favorite a handful of games in. The asymmetrical player powers are so fun to play around with, and the strategical depth is really there, planning ahead is crucial. The variable character powers, random city cards and bonus tiles really makes this game feel different every time. Nice playtime as well, second game (3 player) with two new players (unexperienced in board games) clocked in at 1 hour and 15 min, which is a dream. Depth to game time ratio is near perfect. Oh, and did I mention it's fun as well? Very very fun. It plays extremely well at all player counts, which I love, as our group is small and often it's just 2 of us.

Edit: Playing with the traveling characters (teleporter etc) is TOUGH to me, a lot of planning ahead and every time I try one out, I lose the game. So there are definitely some balancing issues, but they are not as much with the characters, as with players being on the same level.

Edit 2: 15+ plays later, still my number one, replayability is extremely high, and everything just clicks. As close to a perfect 10 as you can get.

RATING: 8
At the start of this game, you are assigned an incredibly strong player power, and are given two cards each with two destinations on the central map. One of the many ways of getting points in this game is by making it to all four destinations, however, in this game, the tightness of resources and the shortage of rounds will make accomplishing even modest goals, very difficult. In fact, I think this is the most claustrophobically tight game I've ever played, which is saying something. Your plans will not work in this game, as you will inevitably be one short of whatever it is you needed, be it gold, camels, money or just one more round. 

To exacerbate this feeling, your workers are all rolled dice, and then they have the strength of action equal to their facing. When you take an action needing multiple dice, you resolve for the lowest value in the lot. Then, in order to resolve an action that someone else has chosen before you, you have to pay money equal to the lowest value in your lot of dice. This means that higher values rolled get you bigger results, but you might wind up not being able to afford those results, so having lower values makes your actions cheaper.  Thankfully the game has rules to allow you to spend the precious camels that you desperately needed to use for other things, on mitigating your dice luck.

 The game has an extremely high amount of variation. There are lots of player powers, all different, the city spaces get populated with randomize cards and there are enough of those that you might not see the same card for many games, and you can randomize the positions of the different income city abilities, to make it so that no path is quite the same twice. The game is an intriguing, but also often frustrating puzzle that I can't decide if I love or hate.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 9
I love clever dice mechanics in eurogames, so this seemed like a no brainer for me (plus, I'm a big fan of "Tzolk'in", so there's also that). And they give you individual player powers too?! I'm sold!

Wonderful eurogame, even if it doesn't do anything new. First of all, it has replayability in spades: the characters, the random action cards in cities, random board composition... It's a Feld-level replayability - each and every game is different, a new puzzle to solve. Not a lot of interaction here, but it's more about optimizing your plan to get the most points, so if you're not a fan of that - avoid. 

It's also quick (40 minutes with two players?), while still giving a feel of a solid eurogame and plays very well at all player counts. Two thumbs up.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of my favourite games. Every play of this game is fun and satisfactory.

Reasons I love this game:
1) Well balanced characters. There is not 1 character that is overpowering than others. All are perfectly well balanced.
2) Thematic. I generally dont care about theme much but while playing Marco Polo, I feel I am part of that world and going through the same ordeal that Marco might have gone through.
3) Dice worker placement. This is one factor that I love and hate about this game. I love it because whatever roll is of dice, there are multiple actions that you can perform.
4) Objectives and contracts. These are the two factors that streamlines the game and avoids it from becoming sandboxy.
5) Deep euro. Even though game is played only over 5 rounds, it is very deep and you can plan your turns ahead. I love it!

Things I dont like/understand:
1) Dice placement. Sometimes, if you consistently roll less value then it can be little difficult to plan your moves. This is not a problem with 2 players but more players, it can become an issue.
2) Blocking of places in 2p. Certain places are blocked in 2p and we did not understand the reason behind it since values of the blocked dice are irrelevant.

That's all I can think about. And honestly, these are inconsequential reasons. The love I have for this game will never ever go.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
27-11-2017
I very much enjoy this game. 
First of all, this game is beautyful. The board, the different coloured dice, the wooden pieces are all beutyfully crafted, it is a joy to look at the board during play. 
The core of the game is its innovative dice placement phase, where each player places dice on different spaces on the board from his own pool (5 dice normally). There are spaces which players can share with one another (albeit with serious cost increase for everyboday except the first player) and there are the big citys on the map, where variable action spaces are set at the beginning of each game, and this spaces can only be used by the players who have trading houses in these cities, which means that the caracthers have to reach these cities during the game if they would like ot use their powers. So based on what (secret) goal cards you have, what are the different action spaces on the cities, how the (variable) bonus tile distribution looks like, you begin the game by planning a rough plan how you would like to win the game. 
Then you spend the rest of the game, trying to realize that plan, fighting with the dice and the other players with the limited action spaces; all this in a very tight economic environment, where every resource is very scarce and the timing of your actions is crucial. 
The icing on the cake are the variable player powers. An on variable I mean VARIABLE. At first sight these powers seems gamebreaking. All of them! And yet, somehow all these different powers make the game ballanced, at the end none of them seem to be overpowered COMPARED TO THE OTHERS. 
Another plus point for me is the great deal of variability in the game: you can change almost everything in this game: the small/big town bonus markers, insta bonuses and action spaces, the characters, your goal cards, just EVERYTHING. 
No two games will be the same based on these criteria.

RATING: 7
After 2 plays, the game is starting to grow on me.  Paradoxically, one of the most interesting aspects of the game, the characters, is also one of its biggest weaknesses.  The characters are fairly well balanced in power level (despite some of the comments you'll see on BGG), but I think they push you too strongly into a single strategic path.  If you are William of Rubruck (you can put trading posts down just by passing through cities), you have to make your whole game about traveling as much as possible.  It would be hard to imagine pursuing a contract strategy with that character.   Other characters take away your ability to really participate in certain aspects of the game.  For example, the character who lets you set the value of each of your dice, takes away an interesting aspect of the game, namely, having to deal with the values that come up on your dice.  

It is definitely a well-designed game, but I'm not sure that it is deserving of all of the awards and praise that it has been receiving of late.  I'd like to play it again, however, and it is possible that my opinion of it will improve.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7
This game is great however it can be demoralizing at times. It's unforgiving.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
(The Voyages of) Marco Polo is a straight forward Euro game, with the action selection mechanism being a dice based worker placement: you roll your dice (=workers) and depening on their number they can the various action with varying effectivity. Higher numbers result in better outcomes, but may make the action more expensive to execute (so higher is not necessarily better).

Other core mechanisms are set collection to fulfill orders. You also turn in your resources to travel the (rather beautiful) map, on which you race to different spots. The game gives you quite a bit of guidance, since your long term goal is to get to Beijing (I know, there are strategies that ignore this), but also give you some direction which way to go (individual goal cities). The cities have a variable setup with different bonus tiles and bonus actions. 

I do not really know why the designers put individual player powers (which have a great effect on gameplay) into the game, especially with some being "easier to play" (is this the new term for "unbalanced") than others. In my view Marco Polo is not a game that I want to sink my teeth into too much to figure out who to play each character most efficiently. I would have preferred these power to be absent, such that you can have an enjoyable and balanced play, when you play Marco Polo from time to time.

I have to add that I am really impressed with the production quality of this game. Each resource has its own unique wooden token (they could just have used cubes instead), the cardboard is very thick and graphic design is both beautiful and functional. To me this is the third game in a row (Russian Railroads, Helios) by Hans-im-Glück which has very nice components.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is a tactical brain-burner, which unfortunately means it also suffers from downtime issues. The random player powers add a nice level of differentiation for the players. I thought the random setup of the board and hidden objectives add some decent replayability to the game. The dice placement seems very restrictive, but the bonus actions allow dynamic space for a player to maneuver and adjust.

I struggled a bit with what I expected going into this game, and what I actually get out of it.

I initially thought, when I first looked into this game, that it was a route building game driven by an interesting dice placement mechanism. To some extent it is, but after a couple plays what I've come to realize is that this game is really more of a difficult resource management game. 

Another thing I thought this game would have is an engine building system. Your route building potentially gives you things at the start of your turn, and you unlock more locations to place dice as the game goes along, which allows you to more easily do things. Unfortunately, I didn't get that feeling while I was actually playing. I guess this probably varies depending on the random card draws to set up the board initially. Even then, building your route is very difficult; it takes a lot of money and/or camels, which can be tricky and time-consuming to come by, especially if there aren't money-making locations on the map. You don't really unlock a lot of abilities until well into the game, and even then things feel nearly as difficult on the final turn as they are on the first.

RATING: 8
(15 plays)


2plays: Awesome awesome awesome, exactly what I was hoping it'd be, based on initial reading. A very strategic game that will require you to bust out the bluprints and analyze every dot, from the get go. I love that... Make a couple of crapola plays, and your booty is fried. The variable characters with special abilities will push you into a certain path, that will most likely reward you with the highest chance of victory. This mind you.. is a premature opinion based on only two plays. The goal cards are quite difficult to complete, if you get cities that are far from each other. Money is tighter than a.. Well, pretty tight! So traveling ain't easy. The game plays soo fast which I can't praise enough, the artwork is phenomenal and the components are premium stuff. Oh, and get those contracts early!! Trust me. All in all, this is a winner and I suspect it'll be a smash hit for the the company. Played two 3p games and it was def better than 2. I'd love to play with four sometime, but I'd be perfectly fine if I play with 3 only from here on. What a game.

I have lowered rating because after 8 games, I am beginning to think that contracts are the the viable strategy. Completing all of your end game cities seems like an after thought. Is is important to travel to certain cities for the actions that they provide yes, but the person that starts amassing early contract points and is able to get ahead, will most likely, based on my experience, always win the game. I don't like that too much. But I like the game a lot, so I'll keep exploring and hopefully prove myself wrong.

Another update: After more plays, I believe that contracts aren't THE viable strategy. A strong path no doubt, but it all depends on the initial board set up and character you're using. Some configurations aligned with certain characters in a way, where travel to certaim places are the key to thrive. Because of the location's specific action, you can make a killing using your chracter's speficic power. Other games, these alignments don't happen or perhaps aren't that obvious, at first. So, your success im this game is based on being the best reader of the board and other player's potential strategy. This is a very very strategic game, esp with two players. Amother thing, contracts are also easier to complete at that count. I imagine with 4, thinking that contracts are your viable strategy, will get you nowhere. And one last thing, this game is so accessible to play because of it's lighning quick playtime. It's a strong reason on why I personally keep coming back to it. Pure excellence and so deserving of it's huge success and current ranking. I cannot wait for my expansion to arrive, those new folks look insanely OP! But just like everyone thought the current ones were OP, am sure they'll be perfectly balanced to co-exist in the game's habitat.

RATING: 7
Lots of forward planning here to make the most of all the town bonuses, your special character ability and all the while looking to beat your opponents to the the spots you need to avoid paying a late fee. Very engaging. Lots to explore as city bonuses and character abilities change from game to game and sometimes with player counts. I'm enjoying it.

Update: Great idea, but the best dice roller usually wins. There's the illusion of control, but a player rolling fives and sixes will normally crush a player rolling threes no matter which character they choose. That said, it's still fun and challenging finding the right combinations to go for, reading the board and finding the right path for your character. So I still rate it highly, but I just don't find the middle level rolls compensated in the same manner as the low roll compensation, and the low roll compensation is paltry too. Hey, it's a dice game after all. Still enjoying it, but hoping to roll high!

Update 2; Find it plays better with three players. Less chaotic, unpredictable, and yet still a good amount of player interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played twice with 3P. Marco Polo is a solid, conventional Euro about collecting resources and using them to either travel or fulfill contracts. Central to the design is a simple mechanism in which sets of dice are played into locations to take the corresponding actions. The only catch is that if there are dice placed in that location already, you have to pay for placement. The other point of interest is that each player has unique abilities which will influence individual decisions throughout.

While the decisions in Marco Polo are tense, the decision space is hardly original. As one player remarked, a lot of the game's replay value hinges upon assessing the relative strength of characters based on the board setup and optimising their abilities. I agree with that sentiment, but that isn't a compelling enough reason for me to revisit the game multiple times.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I have completely re-written this after realizing that I was wrong about my initial negative opinion. The game is great, it just took me three games to realize that. Since then, it gets better with every game played. The options are incredible, but accessible, and the possibilities for developing strategies (long-term goals) and tactics (how to get there best) are great.
The experience is quite overwhelming at the beginninge, and planning is absolutely necessary. The game can be a bit harsh on mistakes, as you need a constant flow of assets to keep going. It gets reasonable quick after one or two games. The options are much clearer then.
At the moment, my favourite game and I want to explore it much more.

---
1 play, 2er. Good, but likely much better with 3 or 4.

A cool worker-placement/action-selection game where the workers are dice (high rolls are good -- stronger action -- but low rolls can be too; can be cheaper to place), the actions require different numbers (1-3) of dice, and you pay money to select an action if another die is already there. And a ton of variety: you only play with subsets of the action cards each game, and the position of each is also random; contracts come out in random order; your goal cards, dealt at start of game, are also random (dealt 4 from a big deck; keep 2). Oh! You also get a unique power for the game! All that randomness in the setup means you'll have a lot to think about at the start of the game, figuring out what sort of strategy to choose, where to move your little dude, what fits best with your power and initial cards, etc.

Neat game, with a lot of "turn zero" thinking but, thankfully, also some randomness (dice rolls) during the game -- so it's not all about the setup! Looking forward to playing with more players.

RATING: 7.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a dice-rolling worker placement game very similar in mechanics to things like Troyes, Alien Frontiers, or Kingsburg.  The main thing that sets this game apart from those similar titles are that each player takes the role of a character with an extremely game-breaking power that adds a great deal of variability and excitement to each game.

As I said, gameplay is pretty standard dice-rolling worker placement fare.  At the start of each round all players will roll their dice and this is their pool of "actions".  Players will then take turns placing their dice on action spaces, some take more than one die, to take the specified action.  One of the neat features here is most spaces provide a benefit equivalent to the number of pips on the die and if multiple dice are played you take the benefit equivalent to the lowest die in that set.  Most places can also be used by multiple players but if you aren't first you need to pay money equal to the number of pips on your lowest die.  This forces players who are late to an action space to make risk/reward decisions since the better the benefit the higher the cost.  This is all in the service of scoring victory points in different ways such as fulfilling orders and traveling across Europe/Asia on a map.

That's nothing new but what is are the character tiles each player is assigned at the start of the game.  Characters and player powers aren't new but these are no minor benefits, they are huge game breaking benefits that completely change the game for different players in different ways.  For example, in my first game my character never rolled dice, I got to choose the number on my dice each time I placed them.  That's huge and immediately other players were concerned it was so much more powerful than theirs.  However I would have actually rather had any other player's character than my own despite how awesome he was.  One player got a resource every time any other player got a resource with their action.  One player never had to pay money to take a used action space.  Another got an extra die and free contract at the start of each turn.  These characters also serve to direct players into a strategy and makes it feel like each player is playing their very own, very different game each time they play.

What is most impressive is how the characters, despite their crazy, sweeping powers, feel balanced.  My first game ended with very close scores.  The spread for the top 3 players in a 4-player game was 3 points.  The art is nice and the components are solid.  Everyone likes to feel all-powerful while not causing balance issues and this game manages that very difficult task.  If I had one negative it is the rulebook.  The rulebook thinks it is helping players by teaching them the game piecemeal and out-of-order giving a broad overview then introducing more complicated and minute rules after.  I don't personally like to learn a game this way but even if I did this makes it difficult for players to find and refer back to specific rules after they do know how to play the game.  All-in-all though that is a minor compliant for a very good worker placement game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Wot i wrote for the Meatballs

'Stravinky’s spring was heralded by the sound of the ice cracking on the Neva river, mine (apart from a bluebell wood walk) is the just opened aroma of a Hans Im Gluck boardgame. The heady cardboard aroma is enhanced by the interior box art, and the comforting Menzel style graphical design. There are no surprises here, its part of the annual cycle of my gaming life. Every year HIG release a middle weight Euro after the Nuremberg fair. All games have a smell, HIG's conjures up images of perfect German families (with the TV switched off - its not even a flat screen), one parent back from their job as an engineer at a factory that makes something, the other perhaps a green energy research scientist. The two children have cycled though fields home from school. They then share the joy of opening the new game and settle down to play.

Some HIG titles don't make far past the rite of box opening. Marco Polo though has made it through 3 games and might be a keeper (Donna likes it). It has nothing original in the game play –you play dice to take actions (mainly get stuff) , supplemented by bonus actions (clever stuff that enhances the main actions) and you fulfil contracts and move around a map gathering more stuff as you go along. You have a character that gives you super powers. There are quite a few of these and their abilities are varied, more variety is added by randomly adding actions and bonuses to the cities your dude travels through retracing Polo’s steps to Beijing. It feels like Terra Mystica in that you choose a character to try and try to work out your puzzle journey from the outset with other players as potential timing nuisances rather than in your face obstructions. However unlike TM it plays quite quickly and the conversions of stuff to VPs don't stretch out to the crack of doom. The box smell lingers, maybe if I move the open box from the games table the magic will be gone. '

RATING: 10
Allthough the theme first tired me: 
Brillant. A mechanical mixture of Terra Mystica and Castles of Burgundy - and as good as these! Nothing left, that a board game can achieve – unless you don't like euros. 

Why?  
(After 2 complete games): 

First game and impression 
Very good. BUT, contracts seemed to be the most effective strategy – travelling seemed not worth while. 

Second game 
With Johannes Caprini (the one, that gets 3 coins and that jumps between oases). The game runs surprisingly. I did fulfill only one (!) contract for the whole game (the starting one), I could use the »jump«-bonus only one time, travelled the (expensive) south route and … won the game in the last round (about 70 points). 

It didn't expect, that this could be possible after the first game. Otherwise I know, that this isn't repeatable; but it shows the variability and unpredictability of the game, depending on the setting. I look forward to other and similar experiences. 

Because of that: not only 8 or 9, but 10 points!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Surprisingly fast and simple HiG euro where players move merchant pawns around a map, scoring points by resource conversion, completing contracts, placing houses in secret cities etc. Actions are triggered by placing dice from a personal pool, but dice can be adjusted or re-rolled (compare Troyes etc.) so this is less random than it sounds.

The game's claim to fame and immediate point of interest is that each player receives a character which seems grotesquely broken in one way or another -- e.g., one has two merchant meeples, one can teleport around the map, one can set dice to any face instead of rolling them. In practice, though, these are all less interesting than they sound: they inform strategy but action costs are such that they are less powerful than they initially appear.

My biggest qualm is that hidden goal cards can actually be quite difficult to achieve. Because the initial setup is random and city cards are uneven, a player able to choose goal cards with good synergy both with each other and with cities they are planning to visit can get an unfair advantage. 

Still, the variety makes it one of the best point-salad-y euros I've played in some time -- likely the best since Russian Railroads.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I expect my rating to move up on subsequent plays.

RATING: 7.5
tl;dr: solid - of course - but not very original

A game from the designers of [thing=126163][/thing] developed by the masters of development at Hans im Glück, of course that showed up on my radar. And as expected, this game works very will indeed. But it is mainly a recycling of well known elements. Use dice to perform actions. Gather resources, travel the map, build settlements, fulfill orders, score points. It's all done slightly different but in a very familiar way.
But there is no denying that even without doing anything really new this is a very solid design than is certainly worth playing if you like strategic middleweight euros.

Initial rating: 7.5/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 8
I enjoy this game very much. Rules are straightforward and easy to grasp (thanks to the great rulebook). Playing flows naturally. The beauty of the game rests in the agony of choices: so much to do, but so little time (5 rounds) and so little resources. The game combines worker placement (where you appoint dice to actions on the board, the pips make how strong your action is, some actions require more than one die to activate), racing (if you want to make it to Beijing first to grasp the 10 points) and variant player powers as each player represents a different historical character with a special ability.
Last pro on my list: game scales well with 2 or more players.

RATING: 9
interesting dice worker placement game about trade and travel in the historic theme of Marco Polo's voyages

There's a lot going on: reaching traveling goals (especially Beijing), fulfilling trading contracts, establishing trading posts that also enable extra action spots or extra income, bonuses for being first in a city, characters with very different game-breaking abilities, dice worker placement with a few twists, extra camel actions to modify dice, getting temporary extra dice, ...

+ game idea with the historical theme tied in
+ asymmetric player powers in a great way(!)
+ board modifications for more variety (city cards, bonus tiles)
+ mechanics
+ great illustration and good materials (though big and small resource pieces are hard to distinguish)
- The name is a bit clunky and ambiguous.
- oddities of the map (e.g. missing important cities)
- a bit complicated (some rule details, hard to keep overview on the board)
- a bit expensive

Such a great game!!

RATING: 10
Incredibly fun worker placement-- difficult to excel at, but not overly punishing. Less direct blocking thanks to the dice-stacking, but still plenty of meaningful considerations to be made thanks to your opponents' play. Filling contracts? Satisfying. Meeting goals? Satisfying. Travelling? Satisfying. The options for player characters really add some fun variability and a nice bit of theme. This is not a "thematic game," but it is a somewhat thematic game. Medieval travel and trade along the Silk Road is admittedly a historical guilty pleasure of mine-- but very few games have done it with such gusto. And smart, fair use of dice? This game was made for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
We dived in the game with a fair amount of anticipation as we liked the designers previous game T'zolkin very much. We were looking forward to a brain burner with a similar depth. The looks of the game (excellent board, custom wooden pieces) and the travelling mechanic also appealed to us.

Unfortunately, we were not impressed. Probably, the dice make or ruin the game for everyone. For us, they were a really annoying mechanism. While there are several ways to modify them or get extra dice to your pool and there are certain catch-up mechanisms in case you were struck by bad luck, still, generally the higher numbers will correspond to more flexibility regarding what you can do. We also liked the fact that as you travel and explore the land you will have more opportunities to place your dice and there is a vast number of possibilities there. At the same time they make the game very unbalanced. There are spots where you can get double the money of your die which is a huge thing in a game where you usually struggle to get money for your expensive travels. In other cases you can generate a couple of victory points which won't change the results of the game much. The special powers should also spice things up, but depending on the journey you are encouraged to take they can be pretty useless in some cases.

The biggest issue with the Voyages of Marco Polo is that the ease of play is missing. On your turn depending on the results of your dice you are taking several extra actions to get money, modify the die roll, hope to get a better die, while there are only so much spaces where you can go and get the necessary resources. This is not an awful game by any means, but very disappointing and certainly not one we would seek out to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A nifty worker-placement game where the workers are dice. With different starting player powers and a variety of location powers that can show up in different spots or not at all from game to game, The Voyages of Marco Polo presents changing tactical challenges and thus loads of replay value. Update: Growing concerns with this one. First, rolling high numbers is categorically better than rolling low numbers, and the cost (in camel expenditures) to mitigate bad rolls is so steep that anyone rolling poorly is double punished. There also seems to be a flaw in the number of black dice available. With four players, there are five black dice, meaning the start player will likely get two of the five. Assuming the start player plows the first die rolled into getting more camels each round, this person can dominate the camels, and thus the black dice (which cost camels) and remain start player almost indefinitely. Add to this the debatable utility of some of the special role powers vs. others and play balance begins to grow to a major issue in my mind. I guess more plays will tell, but I think this game may be interesting but flawed.

RATING: 6
There's nothing really new here, but this is just such a great distillation of euro gameplay. You've got trading in the Mediterranean complete with silk, spices, and gold. So right off the bat it's like they're saying "we're not trying to hide anything, this is a euro game. Take it or leave it."

You've got a type of worker placement in the form of dice placement. You've got a sort of engine building by moving to different cities to get bonuses. And you've got a tight resource economy that consists of balancing your money, camels, and your three main resources. On top of that you've got crazy player powers that change the way you play the game each time. This is is a game all about dice rolling, but one guy doesn't have to roll his dice, he can just use whichever number he wants. This is a game about resource management, but one guy just gets free resources whenever somebody else takes resources. The powers all seem equally overpowered and really make the game tons of fun to play.

The use of dice is excellent here. I really didn't care for Alien Frontiers, I liked Troyes less and less each time I played it, and I thought Bora Bora was just okay. In those games you had a few ways to mitigate the dice, but the dice were frequently frustrating because you just didn't have the thing you needed. In Marco Polo low dice are almost as useful as higher-numbered dice, and there are also plenty of ways to mitigate your dice rolls. If your dice total less then 15, you get money and camels to make up the difference. You can also spend camels to re-roll or adjust your dice, and camels are the resource that is easiest to acquire. It's smart, it works, and unlike in other dice placement games you never feel like you got shafted because of a bad roll.

A few quick items of note: the replayability seems huge. There are (I think) six action spaces that are fixed on the game board, then you put out around 10 extra spaces each game from a deck of 31 cards. So you have a completely different set of actions each game.
The rules are clear and the game is very easy to teach because there are excellent player aids included for each player. Just explain a few things, then walk new players through the player aid.

Update: Having played a few times now, I there seems to be one small issue: the final round is often anticlimactic. Because most of the points in the game are public, there tends to be a runaway leader going into the final round (or even the final two rounds). It seems like this is because the person played better, though, so it doesn't necessarily mean there is a flaw with the game. It reminds me of Istanbul and Lewis and Clark in this area.

The issue is compounded by the lack of player interaction and the inability to completely block any important spaces. In a recent three-player game, for example, we knew by the end of round three that the first-place player was too far ahead to catch. The other two players couldn't do anything to stop him so they basically played for second place.

Sold.

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A euro with variable player powers and a completely unecessery central dice placement mechanism.

The Good: The theme carries through which is neat for a euro. The game is enganging and offers some serious and difficult choices and rewards both tactical and strategic play. The gameplay is tough, tight and thus rewarding when you manage to make some progress. The variable starting player powers is the correct way to go.

The Bad: The theme is still boring for me. The variable starting player powers seem wildly unbalanced, but I need more plays of course to verify that. The most extreme problem for me however is once again the wrong use of dice. I can accept the use of dice as simple crude randomisers in euros (say like in castles of burgundy), but I cannot tolerate them when they are a luck fest. Once again in this game, rolling high is clearly better than rolling low, and there are a ton of mechanisms added to the game to mitigate just that. That is a ton of complexity added to the game for no reason at all other than to make it complex and use the dice like this because I guess it's a fetish.

All in all I'm very dissapointed with Marco Polo. It's a game that could have been a clear, elegant, much simpler and much better if it didn't use a completely unecessery dice system. This is worse than a flawed design, this is a flawed design choice and it loses me almost completely.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I have been looking forward to playing this game quite a bit. I've tried it online, but it's often hard to get a feel for a game when you learn it online. So I was psyched to play it over the board.

The game is a five round game, where you're placing dice to take actions. Generally you can only trigger an action space once per round, and the pip value determines how well you do at that actions. However, if you take the action after another player you'll have to pay the pip value in coins to be able to take that action.

The game is largely about traveling to cities and fulfilling contracts. Traveling lets you do a number of things:

a) get a bonus if you're the first to visit a large city
b) get a bonus every round if you visit a small city
c) have the ability to use a large city special ability
d) place a trading post (there's points if you put out a bunch of them)
e) fulfill your travel goals (you have secret locations you want to visit)
f) get points if you visit Beijing

Much of the game is centered around getting resources to fulfill contracts, money to travel, and camels, which are used to travel, fill contract and also allow you to borrow special black dice for a round.

The game is highly asymmetric with every player having a different special ability. I've heard some rumblings about whether the game is balanced or not because of this, but obviously it's hard for me to have an opinion after a single play. We did have some expansion bits in our game, and I suspect that one of the expansion player powers was unbalanced.

I like this game a lot and hope to play more.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[thing=171623][/thing] is a game of, well, many things. It has dice placement - everyone rolls 5 dice and use them to do the actions in the game. It has route building, as all players receive, in the beginning, two trade routes to connect - by reaching the named cities, you will score points in the end game. It has contracts, which require resources to be fulfilled and, when done, earn the player with points and, maybe, other stuff. It has variable player powers, as every player starts with a character (like Matteo Polo, Johannes Carpini, Berke Khan, and others), which provides an unique ability for the use of the player.

[thing=171623][/thing] borrows a lot from other games and mechanics in order to build an unique whole. And it all worked pretty darn well.

Though higher numbers on dice usually means better actions, and thus rolling (and luck) become an important "skill" in games with dice rolling, [thing=171623][/thing] uses this in smart ways: first, if the combine number of the 5 dice is bellow 15, the player can gain camels or money in a 1:1 basis. Also, in some actions, having a lower number is much preferable - travelling and gaining money are the two most common, but also might apply to the take a contract action. I played with 4 and with 3, and no one playing felt the numbers on the dice determined the outcome of the game, nor when rolling low they don't have what to do in the round. This is pretty great for a dice placement game.

[thing=171623][/thing] seems to have a huge amount of replay value. There are different characters, different routes to build, and the abilities to be used in the cities are randomly set at the start of each game, and there are loads of different abilities. I'm hard pressed to find a game with such a big replay value right out of the box.

The art and graphics are well done (not awesome, but pleasant and fine all around) and do their job. Rarely we had to look for the meaning of the icons on a card. The game is truly language independent.

Downtime, even with 4, wasn't truly an issue, since the player do only one action and passes the turn for the next player. But if a player went for gold (using three dice) than, say, travel (two dice) is possible that she stays out of the round while others still have a lot of actions to do (not only with the 5 normal dice, but also with the black dice, which can be bought). However, this is a choice of the player, and even still, a round will be completely over in around 10 minutes after that.

Strategy is mainly for travel, as it is very important to set goals to where to go and how soon, since arriving first in the big cities give the player a one time bonus, and if it is in Beijing, it will be worth more points the sooner a player gets there (first gain 10 points, second 7, third 4 and fourth 1). Also, in the small cities, players will start to receive, every turn, the benefit from the place, which are usually very good to have. You can also plan for contracts, even though new ones open up every round, there are only 4 resources in the game (gold, silk, pepper and camels), so by having some of them, you will probably be close to made one or more contracts, no matter which ones will open. Overall, smart planning, and knowing when to spend resources (mainly camels) to get more actions (buying a black die) or to change the value of a die, are key to do well in the game, way more than roll high.

There isn't much confrontation in the game - basically is the well known "taking what someone else wants". But even in this [thing=171623][/thing] is light, as for the basic actions (not the ones taken in cities), everyone can use an action already taken, but will have to pay an amount of money equal to the number of the die (or the higher die when using more than one). Though it happened during my plays, for most of the time, no one will have "serious" hard feelings against someone else.

And what about theme? The dreaded downfall of several fine euro games. Well, it isn't the main thing in the game, but it is there. You get resources, you fulfill contracts, you travel, you make trading routes. Travel by sea is costly, and going long distances uses lots of camels. Kublai Khan starts in Bejing, Wilhelm goes evangelizing, Matteo has connections for business, and so on. These make sense. Voyages isn't a thematic game, no, but it works in the level it wants to.

I found [thing=171623][/thing] to be highly enjoyable, easy to play (at least for those used to lots of euros), with little downtime and lots of room for nice plays. This is probably the best low-confrontation euro I got to know this year (with high confrontation, [thing=30645][/thing] is still higher, however, I don't have any doubt about which of them will get way, way more plays one year from now). Highly recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
First impressions:
I am 3 years late for playing this hyped-up SDJ nominee. Was it worth the wait? 
Perhaps. The jury remains out there.

For a 4p game, I felt that VoMP had low rules maintenance. It is a very straight forward,  resource conversion and worker placement Euro using Dice as payment.
Compared to Rajas of the Ganges, another dice placement game, VoMP is less fiddly and more intuitive. The action selection with dice is also a bit more restrictive and there are fewer options on the board. Don't let that fool you though, the game play itself is tight and the variable elements in the game are quite tremendous. We all collectively noted that while certain actions may feel "samey", there are many variable characters, city rewards, contracts and bonuses in the game making it tough to master unless you play the game a lot. Some can see that as a strength, others, a weakness.

A few additional observations: One, the character powers are insane, but feels relatively well-balanced. We all collectively gasped at the powers but enjoyed playing the game with the powers. Without playing these characters, the game would lose a lot of flavor and be relatively bland. Two, traveling is not as easy as it looks. Money is tight, the travel slot can be highly contested and the cities are scattered. This makes traveling TOUGH. It may look like the distance is not insurmountable, but believe me, you won't be moving too much in 5 rounds. 
Three, you need to take advantage of your character power and also find a niche in the game you can exploit because no one else is doing it. That goes without saying for many games, but especially true for VoMP. Fighting for the same resources is suicidal.

After one play, I like but not love the game. It is a game I will gladly play but probably not ask for it often and probably won't be high on my list of immediate replays. Tzolkin is a much better and more unique design.

RATING: 8.2
